# Chapter 1

## CHAPTER 1

Alaric’s mind was a shattered mosaic. A knight’s oath, a lover’s whisper, the iron stink of a battlefield—each shard cut, distinct. Which piece was yesterday? Which was a decade gone? The hearth’s flame held them all, flickering, refusing order. He reached for the wine, his hand trembling not from age, but from the sheer weight of holding a self together.



Footsteps above. Not the scuff of Brother Anselm’s hesitant shuffle, nor the confident slap of the Prior’s soles. These are a stranger’s feet. Measured. Pausing at the turn of the stair. Listening, as I am listening. The blood in my ears is a river in flood, but between its beats, I pluck the sound of a hand on the iron ring of the door. The grate of the hinge is a scream I feel in my teeth.

I do not open my eyes. The vision of the undercroft is painted on the inside of my lids: the three barrels of salt fish, the rack of empty wine casks like a giant’s honeycomb, the hessian sacks of meal, the dark arch that leads to the older crypts where the bones are stacked. I see it clearer than if I looked. I am the spider in the corner of the ceiling. I am the grain in the wood of the barrel. I am the space between the stones.

“You are here.” The voice is not a question. It is flat, a stone dropped into a well. It is not a monk’s voice. It has no chant in it, no rise and fall of prayer. It is the voice of a man who counts things and finds the count correct.

I let my breath out, a slow leak. I am Simon of the Wood, once. I am the lay brother who mucks the stables and grinds the grain. I am a vessel, mostly empty. I open my eyes.

He stands in the doorway, a slice of grey daylight at his back. He is tall, but not a giant. His cloak is good, dark wool, no trim. His boots are scarred but solid. His face is all angles, as if carved by a hurried hand. No beard. A soldier’s face, or a bailiff’s. His eyes find me in the gloom as if he knew the exact shelf I was stored on.

“I am here,” I say. My voice is the rustle of straw. It surprises me, that it still works.

“Get up.”

I push myself up. The stone *was* cold. I was wrong. The chill is in my bones, not the rock. It floods back into me, a tide of ache. I stand, a little crooked. The world tilts, rights itself. The sweet-rot smell is stronger. It is coming from the sack by the fish barrels. Apples. I was right.

The man does not move. He is assessing. Not my strength, I have little. Not my threat, I am none. He is reading the text of my wear, the grime under my nails, the way my knees bend inward like an old gate.

“You are to come,” he says.

“The Prior…”

“The Prior has sold your labour for a week. A debt. You are mine for seven days.”

A week. The word unfolds in my head. Seven dawns. Seven nights. A string of beads to count. Outside these walls. The thought is too large. It has no edges. I break it into smaller pieces. The walk to the gate. The track through the wood. The ford at the river. One piece at a time. Otherwise, the world spills.

“Where?” I manage.

“To the manor. To the house.” He says it as if I should know. As if everyone knows the house. “There is a thing to be done. A thing remembered.”

I look at my hands. They are remembering the feel of a hoe’s shaft, the rough hair of a horse’s neck, the gritty texture of milled grain. They do not remember any other thing.

“I remember nothing,” I say. It is my truth. It is the wall I live behind.

A flicker in his stone face. Not a smile. A crack. “You will,” he says. “Or you will be of no use. And useless things are paid for differently.”

He turns. The conversation is a closed door. I am to follow. I take a step. My footfall is too loud in the vault. I am a clumsy thing, full of noise. I pass the sack of apples. The smell is a hook in my brain. It pulls up a colour: the green of a sun-dappled orchard, the weight of a fruit in a small hand. A flash, then gone. A memory that is not mine. They come sometimes, like bats from a cave. I do not chase them. They bite.

Up the stairs, the light grows. It is a watery, spring light. It hurts. At the top, the world reassembles itself in a riot of sensation. The cloister walk is a frame of sky. The grey is not flat; it is a thousand shades, bruised and soft. The sound of a bell being washed in a bucket. The scrape of a hoe in the kitchen garden. The sigh of the wind in the yew tree. It is too much. I have been in the quiet dark too long. I am a raw nerve.

Brother Anselm is by the refectory door, his hands tucked into his sleeves. His eyes are wet stones. He looks at me, then at the man, then at the ground. He is ashamed. He sold me. Or the Prior did. A debt. I am a sack of meal, a side of bacon. A thing to settle an account.

“Simon,” Anselm says. My name is a plea.

I nod. I have nothing to give him. No forgiveness, no anger. They are both too heavy to carry.

The man walks, and I walk behind him, a duckling made of rags and bones. We pass through the gatehouse. The porter does not look up from his bowl of pottage. We are already gone. The track is mud and stone. The forest leans in on both sides, a wall of still-bare branches and deep green firs. The air is different. It moves. It carries the smell of wet earth, of rotting leaves, of something alive and sharp.

I walk. One foot. The other foot. The rhythm of it starts to empty my head. This I can do. This is a kind of prayer. The man walks ahead, silent. His back is a shield. I watch his heels strike the ground. I match my pace to his. We are two beads on a string, sliding through the wood.

The river ford is ahead, the water chuckling over the stones. He stops, turns. “You know this place?”

I look. The water is the colour of sky. The stones are large, moss-grown. On the far bank, an alder tree leans out, its roots clawing the air. A jolt goes through me. A physical thing, like a shiver, but inside my ribs. I *know* the pattern of those roots. I know the sound this water makes when it is high and angry. I know the cold bite of it around my ankles.

“I have crossed it,” I say. The words are not mine. They come from the water, from the moss.

“Before the abbey?”

I look at the roots of the alder. There is a hollow there, behind the curtain of them. A child could hide. *I* hid. The memory is not an image. It is a feeling: damp wool, heart hammering against my knees, the taste of fear like a copper coin on my tongue. Hiding from shouts. From a voice, roaring.

I shut it down. I build the wall. Stone by stone. I am Simon of the Wood. I grind the grain. I tend the horse. I have no before.

“I remember nothing,” I say, the familiar chant.

The man’s eyes are on me. He saw the jolt. He is a hunter. He has seen the grass move. “You will,” he says again, and turns to cross the ford.

The water is achingly cold. It soaks through my worn boots in an instant. The current tugs, playful and strong. My feet, on the slick stones, remember their own balance. They find holds I do not see. I cross without stumbling. On the other side, I look back. The abbey is gone, swallowed by the trees and the fold of the land. There is only the river, the track, the forest.

And the house.

It appears as we round a bend: a steep pitch of slate roof, a jumble of timber and plaster walls, a stone chimney stack shouldering into the sky. It is not a castle. It is a fortified manor, old and proud and sinking a little into the earth. The moat is a thick band of still, dark water, choked with reeds. The wooden bridge is down. The gate in the palisade is open.

The man stops. “Hawksford,” he says. It is not for me. It is a name laid on the air, a key in a lock.

Hawksford. The word does not fit in my head. It is too big, too sharp-edged. It will not go on the shelf with the other words. It rolls around, cutting me.

We cross the bridge. The moat water smells of stagnation and frogs. A duck erupts from the reeds, wings clattering. My heart clatters with it. Inside the palisade is a yard of churned mud. A scrawny chicken pecks at nothing. A boy with a snot-crusted face stares from a doorway. The house looms. Its windows are eyes, shuttered.

The great door is oak, iron-bound. The man does not go to it. He goes around the side, to a smaller door. The kitchen entrance. The realm of work. He pushes it open.

Heat and smell wallop me. A fire roars in a vast hearth. A cauldron bubbles. The air is thick with the smell of onions, herbs, smoke, and the tang of unwashed bodies. A woman kneads dough at a table, her arms white to the elbow. A boy turns a spit, his face glowing with sweat and firelight. They all stop. They look at the man, then at me.

A large woman, her face red and kind, wipes her hands on her apron. “Master Reynard. You have him.”

Master Reynard. The man has a name. Reynard the Fox. It suits him.

“This is Simon,” Reynard says. “He works. He eats. He sleeps in the stable loft. He speaks to no one of his business here. His business is mine.”

The woman nods. Her eyes on me are curious, but not unkind. “He’s half-starved, Master Reynard. Look at him.”

“Feed him then, Marta. Then bring him to the hall. My lord wishes to see him.”

Reynard leaves. The door shuts, and I am alone in the press of the kitchen heat. The spit-boy snickers. The dough-woman, Marta, clicks her tongue. “Sit,” she commands, pointing to a stool by the hearth.

I sit. The heat is a physical force. It melts the chill in my bones, turns it to a damp sweat on my skin. She brings me a bowl. It is a thick stew, with bits of barley, turnip, and shreds of meat. A hunk of coarse bread. I hold the bowl. The warmth soaks into my hands.

“Eat, poor ghost,” Marta says.

I eat. The taste is a violence. Salt, fat, earth, fire. It is too much. My stomach, shrunken to a fist, clenches in protest. I force it down. Each spoonful is a memory of other food, abbey food: bland, thin, a sustenance without joy. This is different. This is life, and death, in a wooden bowl.

As I eat, the memories come. Not as pictures, but as echoes in the taste. The taste of wild garlic picked in a ditch. The taste of a stolen egg, eaten raw behind a barn. The taste of blood from a split lip. They are not my memories. They are the memories of the body. The animal that lived before the abbey. The ghost she called me.

When the bowl is empty, I am more solid. The world has edges again. Marta takes the bowl. “Come,” she says. “Best not keep them waiting.”

She leads me out of the kitchen, down a passage of rough stone. The air cools. We come to another door. She pushes it open.

The hall is vast, dark, smoky. High windows let in slants of grey light that cut through the haze. The floor is rushes, old and sweet-smelling from herbs. A fire burns in a central hearth, the smoke rising to a blackened roof. At the far end, on a dais, is a table.

A man sits there. He is old. His hair is white, a wild halo. His face is a landscape of ravines and cliffs. He is wrapped in a fur robe, though the hall is not cold. His hands, on the table, are twisted with age, but large. They look like they could still break a thing.

Beside him stands Reynard. And on the other side, a younger man. Handsome, clean, with a bored, petulant mouth. He wears a fine blue tunic. He looks at me as if I were a dung beetle that has crawled onto his plate.

Marta nudges me forward, then retreats, closing the door.

I stand in the rushes. The old man’s eyes are on me. They are a pale, watery blue. They see everything and nothing.

“Come closer,” the old man says. His voice is the sound of dry reeds rubbing together.

I walk forward. The rushes whisper under my feet. I stop before the dais. I look at the ground.

“Look at me.”

I raise my head. His eyes hold me.

“They tell me you remember nothing,” the old man says. “Is this true?”

“It is true, my lord.”

“I am Sir William de Hawksford,” he says. “This is my son, Geoffrey.” The younger man gives a minute, disdainful nod. “And you know Master Reynard. He is my… rememberer.”

I say nothing.

“There is a chest,” Sir William says. His twisted hand makes a vague gesture towards the shadows of the hall. “An iron-bound chest. It is in this house. It has been in this house for a very long time. It belongs to me. It contains a document. A proof. Of land. Of a right.”

He pauses, his breath a shallow rattle. “I have forgotten where I put it.”

Geoffrey shifts impatiently. “Father, this is absurd. A dusty monk’s-get, a simpleton…”

“Silence!” The old man’s voice cracks like a whip. Geoffrey flinches. “I did not *misplace* it, you fool. I hid it. In my prime. When I saw what vipers I had bred. I hid it where no one would think to look. Where only a man who thinks as I thought then could find it.” His eyes burn into me. “I have forgotten the hiding place. But my mind has not forgotten. It is in there.” He taps his own temple with a bony finger. “Locked away. The key is broken.”

I understand nothing. “My lord, I am not a key.”

“You are a blank parchment,” Reynard says, his flat voice cutting the air. “You remember nothing of your own life. Your head is empty. We will fill it with his. With the memories of this house, of that time. You will walk where he walked. You will see what he saw. You will *become* the vessel for his lost thought. And you will find the chest.”

The absurdity of it is a wave that should make me laugh. But the hall is too dark, their faces too grave. They mean it. They have bought me for a week to perform a miracle of memory.

“How?” is all I can say.

Sir William leans forward, a strange light in his old eyes. “We begin at the beginning. You will sleep in my old chamber. You will wear an old tunic of mine. You will handle the things I handled. Reynard will tell you stories. I will tell you fragments. We will pour my past into your empty cup. And perhaps, God willing, it will spill out the one thing we need.”

He sinks back, exhausted. Geoffrey looks murderous. Reynard’s face is unreadable.

I am a blank parchment. I am a vessel. I am a ghost to be filled with another man’s ghost. The stone of the undercroft was not cold. This is cold. This is the deep, dry cold of the tomb they are asking me to climb inside.

“Tonight,” Sir William whispers, his eyes drifting to the smoke-blackened rafters. “We begin tonight. Start with the blood. He should know about the blood on the stairs.”

The fire pops. A log settles. The rushes seem to hold their breath.

And in the silence, I feel it: the first, faint echo. Not a memory. A pressure. As if the walls of this hall, the stones of this house, are leaning in, waiting to tell their story. And they will use my tongue to tell it.



---

# Chapter 4

## CHAPTER 4

The bed is not soft. That is the first wrong thing. The bed in the high chamber should be a mound of feathers, a sigh of straw, a thing that accepts the shape of a man. This bed is a slab of clouded memory. The linen smells of lavender, but the lavender is old, a scent pressed between pages of a book left in the sun. It smells of effort. Of trying to remember what comfort was. I lie on my back and feel the ropes beneath the thin mattress, a grid of taut lines holding me above the floor. Sir William’s tunic is rough on my skin. It is too broad across the shoulders, too short in the sleeve. It does not feel like a garment. It feels like a shed skin, empty and waiting for a new thing to fill it.

Footsteps outside. Not Reynard’s silent glide, nor Geoffrey’s angry stalk. These are servant feet, quick and light, carrying something that clinks. They pass. The house breathes around me. It is a lung of stone and old timber, inhaling the night, exhaling the dust of sixty years. I am to sleep here. I am to dream here. They are pouring his past into my empty cup. The cup is cracked. I feel the leak, a slow seep of myself into the rough wool of his tunic, into the grid of the bed-ropes. What is left of me? A name they gave me. A body that aches. A head full of other people’s echoes.

I close my eyes and see the blood on the stairs.

Not my memory. His. Sir William’s telling of it, poured into me an hour ago in the firelit hall. A telling that was not words, but a thing he carved out of the air with his twisted hands.

*It was not a great wound,* he had said, his eyes not on me but on the stairwell in the corner of the hall. *A gash from a careless scythe, brought in on a harvest-hand’s boot. Just a slick, a whisper of it on the third step from the bottom. We missed it in the dark. My brother Hubert stepped in it first. Left a perfect print, heading up. Then my father. Then me. We tracked it through the screens passage, into the hall. We painted the house with it, and never knew. Not until morning, when the light showed the trail. A family, walking in a stranger’s blood.*

I open my eyes. The ceiling is black with smoke-shadow. The story is in me now. It is not an image. It is a sensation. The slight give of a half-dried slick under a leather sole. The faint, iron smell rising in the cool dawn air as the sun hit the steps. The shame of it. The intimate violation. The house, marked.

That is how it begins. Not with a chest. With a stain.

***

**Scene 1**

The dawn comes as a grey rumour at the narrow window. I have not slept. The bed’s grid is printed on my back. When I rise, the room is a cavity of shadows. There is a ewer of water, a basin. The water is cold. It should be cold. This, at least, is right. I splash my face and the shock of it is mine, a clean, present moment. I dry on a rough linen cloth that smells of thyme.

Reynard is at the door when I open it. He is a stain of black wool in the gloom of the passage. He does not speak. He turns and I am meant to follow. We do not go to the hall. We go down, into the belly of the house.

The undercroft is different by day. Slants of dusty light cut through high, narrow vents. They lay bare the floor: not smooth flags, but a sea of packed earth and rushes, littered with the detritus of storage—splintered barrel staves, a rusting hinge, a single child’s shoe, leather curled like a dead leaf. The stone *is* cold here. The chill rises through my thin soles. This is the correct cold. I feel a perverse satisfaction.

“Here,” Reynard says. His voice is flat, a tool for pointing. He stands before a section of wall. It is bare stone, large blocks, slick with damp in the seams. “He played here. As a boy. He hid here during games of hunt-the-hare.”

I look. It is a wall. I feel nothing. I am a blank parchment. I wait for the writing.

“Tell me what you see,” Reynard says.

I see stone. I see damp. I see a spider’s web, jewelled with moisture, in the corner of two blocks. “A wall.”

“Look with his eyes.”

“I don’t have his eyes.”

“You are wearing his skin.” Reynard does not gesture to the tunic. The statement is a fact, bare as the wall. “Look again.”

I try. I try to un-see my own seeing. I try to imagine a boy’s shape crouching in this gloom, breath held, heart a rabbit in his chest. I try to feel the thrill of hiding. Nothing comes. There is only the wall, and the damp, and the weight of Reynard’s expectation. It is a small test. I am failing it.

“What did he feel here?” I ask, a surrender.

“Safety,” Reynard says. A simple word. “The stone was close. It held him. He could hear the seekers’ feet on the flags above, but here, in the dark, he was swallowed. He was part of the house’s bones.”

I place my palm on the stone. It is cold and wet. I try to feel swallowed. I feel only a man with a damp hand.

“It’s not working,” I say.

“It is day one,” Reynard says. He turns from the wall. “The mind is not a door to be kicked in. It is a lock to be picked. We try a different pin. Come.”

We move through the undercroft. He shows me things. A worn post where, he says, William’s father used to tie his favourite hound. A scar on the floor where a barrel of cider burst, and they all got drunk licking it up. A scratch on a beam, a tally of days a prisoner was kept here during some forgotten feud. Each fact is laid before me like an exhibit. Each is a pebble dropped into the well of my emptiness. I hear the plink. I wait for the splash, the echo. There is only the dry sound of the pebble hitting bottom.

We ascend to the kitchen. It is a vault of heat and chaos after the undercroft. A fire roars in a hearth wide enough to roast an ox. A scullion turns a spit, his face shining with grease and sweat. A cook pounds herbs in a mortar, the *thump-thump-thump* a frantic heartbeat. The air is thick with the smells of onion, smoke, burning fat.

A young woman is kneading dough at a flour-dusted table. She is maybe eighteen, sleeves rolled to her elbows, arms strong and pale. She sees Reynard and her hands still for a moment, then resume their work, a little harder. Her eyes flick to me. They are the colour of slate after rain.

“Marta,” Reynard says. “This is our guest. He is to have the freedom of the house.”

Marta nods, a tight dip of her chin. Her gaze is assessing, not unkind, but wary. She looks at Sir William’s tunic on me, and her lips press thin. She knows. The whole house must know. The ghost-walker. The memory-thief.

“He’ll need to eat,” Reynard says. “See he gets what he needs.”

He leaves me there, standing like a post in the middle of the kitchen’s storm. The small test is over. I have failed it. Now I am in a new world, and its rules are a mystery. The scullion sneers at my tunic. The cook ignores me entirely. Marta works her dough, folding, pressing, turning.

I stand. I am a blank parchment in a room full of shouting ink.

After a time, she speaks without looking up. “You’re in the way.”

I step aside, towards the hearth. The heat is immense.

“Not there. You’ll singe.”

I step back.

She sighs, a short, sharp sound. “Sit.” She nods to a three-legged stool in the corner, near a basket of turnips. “If you’re to be underfoot, be still underfoot.”

I sit. The stool wobbles. I hold still. I watch her work. The dough is a living thing under her hands. She is not gentle with it. She slams it, folds it, attacks it. It is a battle, and she is winning. Flour dusts the air around her like a halo.

“You’re the one, then,” she says. “The one to find the old knight’s treasure.”

“I’m to try.”

She barks a laugh, humourless. “A week. For a thing lost since before my granny was born. They’re mad.”

“They are desperate.”

She looks at me then, really looks. Her eyes take in the ill-fitting tunic, the hollows under my eyes, the way my hands rest on my knees—not quite belonging to the body they’re attached to. “You don’t remember anything? Truly?”

“Truly.”

“Must be peaceful.”

It is the first thing anyone has said that approaches understanding. “It is not peace,” I say. “It is… a room with no walls. The wind blows straight through.”

She considers this. Her hands slow. “And they’re trying to build walls out of his life.”

“Yes.”

“Fool’s work.” She goes back to kneading. But the violence has gone out of it. It is just work now. “Still. A paying job is a job. Even a strange one. You want bread? It’ll be a while.”

“I can wait.”

I sit on the wobbling stool. She works. The kitchen swirls around us. I am not remembering Sir William. I am watching Marta. The fine hairs on her forearms, white with flour. The stubborn set of her jaw. The efficient, unthinking grace of her movements. This is now. This is real. It is a pinprick of light in the fog of borrowed memory. I cling to it.

She cuts the dough, shapes it into loaves. “You’ll need to do something,” she says, not looking at me. “Sitting there like a lost soul just makes you a target for gossip. And worse. Geoffrey’s lot already think you’re a fraud or a witch. Probably both.”

“What should I do?”

“Make yourself useful. Here.” She shoves the basket of turnips towards me with her foot. “Peel. If you’re to haunt my kitchen, you can work.”

She tosses a knife onto the table. It lands with a clatter. A small, sharp test. I pick up the knife. I pick up a turnip. The skin is tough, earth-caked. I begin to peel. The first strokes are clumsy. The peel comes off in thick, wasteful chunks.

She watches from the corner of her eye. Says nothing.

I find a rhythm. The blade bites, follows the curve. The pale flesh emerges. It is a simple, mindless task. My hands know it, even if I do not. The peel falls in a long, continuous curl into the basket at my feet. I am doing something. I am not a vessel. I am a man peeling a turnip.

“Not completely useless, then,” Marta says, and there might be the faintest thread of warmth in it.

It is a small victory. The first one. I feel the ghost of a smile on my own face. It feels strange, like a muscle long unused. I keep peeling.

***

**Scene 2**

The loaves are in the oven, the turnips peeled. Marta has given me a hunk of cheese and some yesterday’s bread. I eat in the kitchen corner, the food tasting of smoke and real hunger. The scullion, a boy named Tom, has stopped sneering. He watched me peel. Efficiency, it seems, is a language he understands.

Reynard finds me there. He does not comment on the peelings, on the knife at my side. “Come. Sir William wishes to walk.”

We find the old knight in the herb garden, a cloistered square within the inner walls. He leans on Geoffrey’s arm, but his face is turned to the weak sun. He looks frail as a dried leaf. Geoffrey’s expression is stone.

“Ah,” Sir William says, his eyes finding me. They are milky, but alert. “The vessel. Has Reynard begun the filling?”

“He has shown me the undercroft.”

“And?”

“The stones are cold there.”

Sir William’s mouth twitches. “Good. That is your memory now. Hold it. The cold of those stones is a fact of my childhood. It is now a fact of yours.” He pats Geoffrey’s arm. “Leave us. Let the vessel walk with me.”

Geoffrey’s jaw tightens. “My lord, you are not steady.”

“I am steady enough to fall. Go. Tend to your men. They look restless at the gates.”

With a last, venomous glance at me, Geoffrey departs. His dislike is a solid thing, a wall I can lean against. It is more real than Sir William’s memories.

The old man takes my arm. His grip is surprisingly strong, a bundle of bird-bones and wire. We walk slowly along the gravel path between beds of rosemary, thyme, sage. The scents are clean, medicinal.

“He thinks this is madness,” Sir William says quietly. “He is a good captain. He loves this house. He sees it dying. A slow death by debt and dry rot. The chest… it is a last hope. Not just for gold. For a… a *reason*. A thing my father hid that was worth the hiding. Proof we were not always a fading line on a forgotten parchment.”

We walk. His weight is slight. “I don’t know how to find it,” I say.

“You are not *you* finding it,” he says, impatient. “It is *me* finding it. The me that is locked in this old, broken body. We are… bypassing the break. Using your sound mind as a bridge to the past. Do you understand?”

“I understand the theory. The practice is silence.”

He stops, turns to face me. The sun is in his eyes, making them seem to swim with pale light. “Tell me of the blood on the stairs.”

I hesitate. The story is there. I recite it. “A harvest-hand’s boot. A gash. A slick on the third step. You tracked it through the house. Hubert’s print, then your father’s, then yours.”

He is listening, but not to the words. He is listening for something behind them. “And what did the blood smell like?”

I falter. He didn’t say. “I… don’t know.”

“Think. The stairs were stone. The blood was half-dried by morning. In the cool air of the passage. What did it smell like?”

I close my eyes. I am not reaching for his memory. I am reaching for the memory of his telling. The hall. The fire. His voice. I smell the woodsmoke. I smell the rushes. And then, like a faint thread through it… “Iron,” I say. “And… dust. And something else. Something sweet-sour. The rushes, maybe. Rotting where the blood soaked in.”

Sir William’s hand tightens on my arm. His breath catches. “Yes,” he whispers. “The rushes. We changed them that very day. Burned them. My mother insisted. She said the house had tasted blood and would want more.” He looks at me, a fierce, hungry look. “You see? It is in there. You have the scent. The key is not broken. It is rusty. We must oil it.”

We resume walking. He is excited now, a frail energy animating him. He points with his free hand. “That rosemary bush. My mother planted it. She said it was for remembrance. I broke a branch off once, to put in my sister’s coffin. She was stillborn. A tiny thing. The rosemary was too big for her hands.” He stops, his voice thickening. “The smell of it… it was the smell of that week. Death and rosemary.”

I look at the bush. It is gnarled, ancient. I smell the sharp, pine-like scent on the air. And now, when I smell it, I will not think of my own nothing. I will think of a tiny coffin, and a stolen branch. He is building his past inside me, brick by brick. I am the site of a new construction. It feels less like filling a cup, and more like being hollowed out to make room.

We are joined by others. Brother Anselm appears, drawn by the sun, his prayer book in hand. He gives me a timid nod. Then Marta comes, bringing a cup of watered wine for Sir William. She stays, walking a little behind us, her arms crossed over her flour-dusted apron.

Sir William talks. He is a stream of fragments. *This is where my dog, Troilus, dug for a bone and found a Roman coin. This is where I first kissed a girl—a dairy maid named Bess, and she tasted of milk and summer. This is where my father told me I would not be a knight, that the world had no need of another poor swordsman, that I must learn the land instead.*

We listen. Brother Anselm murmurs a prayer sometimes. Marta is silent, her face unreadable. I am a blank parchment, and the pen is scratching, scratching.

It is not a formal council. It is not a planned alliance. But a group is forming. Around the old man’s fading light. Around the absurd quest. Around me, the empty centre. Anselm, the keeper of faith. Marta, the keeper of the hearth. Reynard, the keeper of the method. And Sir William, the keeper of the lost thing.

We are bound by a shared challenge: to make a ghost walk. To make a memory sing in a stranger’s throat. We walk the herb garden, and for a moment, the house does not feel like a tomb. It feels like a thing we are all holding up, together, against the weight of time and debt.

***

**Scene 3**

The illusion shatters at supper.

We are in the hall. The high table has Sir William, Geoffrey, Reynard, and a nervous-looking steward named John. I am given a place at the lower table, with Anselm and Marta. The hall is half-full—household guards, a few senior servants. The mood is subdued. The bread is good. Marta’s bread.

Geoffrey’s voice cuts across the murmur. He is not speaking to me. He is speaking to Sir William, but it is meant to carry.

“...a week of this mummery, my lord. The men talk. They see a stranger in your clothes, listening to old tales, and they think the mind of the house is slipping. They need confidence. They need to see strength. Not… archaeology.”

Sir William pushes a piece of meat around his trencher. “The strength of this house is in its past, Geoffrey. We have little else.”

“The strength of this house is in its walls, and the men who guard them!” Geoffrey’s fist rests on the table. “We are not a library. We are a fortress. Or we were. This… this *digging* in old wounds and older dust. It is a distraction. The real threat is at our gates. Not in our memories.”

“What threat?” Sir William asks, weary.

“Baron de Clare’s factors. They ride the boundaries. They ask questions in the village. They look at our fields, our mills, our timber, and they see a property already slipping into their hands. They do not wait for ghosts to find treasure. They make their own.”

A chill settles over the table. This is the clear enemy. Not a shadowy memory. A living, grasping power. Debt is their weapon. Time is their ally.

Reynard speaks, his voice cool oil on the heated air. “The treasure, if found, would remove the debt. It would fortify the walls better than a hundred guards.”

“*If*,” Geoffrey scoffs. “A word thinner than hope. And in the meantime, we look feeble. We look like we believe in fairy stories.” His eyes find me, down the table. They are the colour of flint. “We put our faith in a man with no past.”

All eyes turn to me. The bread turns to clay in my mouth.

Marta speaks beside me, low but clear. “He peels a mean turnip, Captain. That’s a present skill.”

A few stifled laughs from the lower table. Geoffrey’s face darkens.

“A useful talent for a kitchen maid. Not for the salvation of a house.”

“Enough, Geoffrey,” Sir William says, but the wind has gone from him.

“No, my lord. I will not have enough.” Geoffrey stands. He addresses the room. “You all know the situation. You know the wolves are circling. We have a week. A week of this… experiment. I will not stand in my lord’s way. His will is law.” He turns his flint-eyes back to me. “But when the week is done, and the chest is still lost, we will face reality. We will sell what we must. We will shrink back to the keep, if we can hold it. And we will do it as men. Not as children listening for whispers in the walls.”

He sits. The silence is absolute. The antagonistic ideology is laid bare: memory is weakness. The past is a fairy story. The only truth is the sword, the wall, the coin in hand. It is a world of solid, unforgiving things. A world where I am nothing.

Sir William is pale. He looks at me, a plea in his milky eyes. *Prove him wrong.*

But I am a blank parchment. The ink they are using is faint, watery. It is no match for Geoffrey’s carved-stone certainty.

I look down at my trencher. I see the blood on the stairs. Not as a story. As a stain. A stain tracked through the house by unknowing feet. That is what we are doing. Tracking a ghost-stain. And Geoffrey is right. The wolves at the gate do not care about stains. They care about meat.

The shared challenge that bonded us in the herb garden now feels fragile, a spider’s web in the path of a boot. The enemy is not just de Clare. It is the despair that lives in Geoffrey’s voice. It is the belief that the story is over.

***

**Scene 4**

I do not sleep. The bed’s grid presses into me. The house is silent, but it is a listening silence. I hear the blood on the stairs. I smell the rosemary by the tiny coffin. I feel the cold of the undercroft stones. Sir William’s memories are like mice in the walls of my mind, scratching, nesting.

An idea comes. It is not a memory. It is a thought of my own, rising through the poured-in past like a bubble through mud. It is a simple thought.

*If I am to be his vessel, I must be empty of me.*

But I am not empty. I am full of the present. The wobble of the stool. The weight of the knife. The feel of turnip skin giving way. Marta’s assessing eyes. Geoffrey’s contempt. These are my memories. They are in the way.

I rise. I put on the rough tunic. I leave the chamber. The corridor is black, but a grey smear of moonlight comes from the high window at the end. I know where I am going. Not to the undercroft. Not to the herb garden.

To the stairs.

The main staircase from the hall to the solar wing. They are wide, wooden, worn into dips by generations of feet. I stand at the bottom. The third step.

I close my eyes. I try to silence my own mind. The criticism of the day, the fear, the solid, present ache in my shoulders from the bed. I push it down. I am a blank parchment.

*Blood on the stairs.*

I do not think of the story. I try to *become* the boy coming down in the morning light. The shock of seeing the dark, rust-brown slick. The confusion. The slow-dawning horror as the trail leads away, through the passage…

I take a step. My foot falls on the third step. I imagine the slight, tacky give.

Nothing.

I am a man standing on a step.

Frustration rises, hot and sharp. This is folly. Geoffrey is right. I am a fraud. I open my eyes. The moon paints the steps in silver and black. And then I see it.

Not with my eyes. With the back of my neck. A pressure. A sense of *pattern*.

The stairs are not just stairs. They are a cascade of shadows. The moon through the arrow-slit window casts the balustrade’s shadow across them in a series of repeating, diagonal bars. Like the grid of the bed. Like the lines on a parchment. The third step is cut in half by a bar of darkness.

I move without thinking. I crouch. I run my fingers along the edge of that shadow, where it meets the wood of the step. The wood is smooth, worn. But there, under my fingertips, at the very edge of the shadow-line… a faint, irregularity. A series of tiny, shallow nicks in the wood. Deliberate. Too straight to be wear.

My heart is a drum in the quiet. This is not a memory. This is *now*. I trace the nicks. One, two, three… seven. Seven little cuts, hidden in the shadow’s edge. A tally.

A prisoner’s tally? A child’s game?

I do not know. But it is a thing. A real, physical thing that is not in the stories Reynard told. It is a secret in the wood.

I hear a footfall on the rushes below. I freeze, my hand still on the step.

Marta stands at the bottom of the stairs, a shawl over her nightdress, a candle in her hand. Her face is all shadow and flickering gold. “Couldn’t sleep either?”

I cannot speak. I point to the nicks.

She climbs a few steps, leans in. Her candle illuminates them. Seven little scars in the old oak. “What are they?”

“I don’t know.” My voice is a whisper. “But they’re hidden. In the shadow-line, at this time of night. You’d only see them if you were looking… if you knew to look.”

She looks from the nicks to my face. “His?”

“I think so. A thing he made. A thing he never told.”

We stare at the little marks. They are nothing. They are everything. They are a crack in the smooth surface of the lost past. A real thing, found. Not by his memory, but by my seeing. By the combination: his story of the blood on *this* step, and my eyes, guided by the moon’s geometry.

It is not the chest. It is a preliminary victory. A tiny one. But it is solid. It is a fact.

“Seven,” Marta murmurs. “Days? Weeks? Sins?”

“Loves?” I suggest, the word out before I can stop it.

She snorts, but it is a soft sound. “A hopeful ghost, then.” She straightens. “Come away. If Geoffrey finds you caressing the staircase in the dark, he’ll have you in the undercroft for real.”

I follow her down. The cold of the hall stones seeps through my soles. The correct cold.

“Does it feel different?” she asks. “Now that you’ve found a piece of him?”

I think. The nicks in the wood are real. They are a hook in the fog. I am no longer just a vessel. I am an archaeologist. I have uncovered a fragment. The memory-identity is not just poured in; it is being dug up, jointly, by his clues and my labour.

“Yes,” I say. “It feels… like a shared secret. Between him, and me, and the house.”

She looks at me, her candle painting her face in warm and moving light. “Just don’t let the house tell you all its secrets at once. Some of them might be heavy.”

She turns to go, then pauses. “The bread for tomorrow’s breakfast. It needs to be started. The dough won’t knead itself.”

It is not an invitation. It is a statement of fact. But it is an opening.

I nod. “I can peel something.”

A ghost of a smile touches her lips. “I’m sure you can.”

I watch her light recede into the kitchen passage. The hall is vast and dark around me. But the cold is correct. And in my mind, clear as the seven nicks in the wood, is the beginning of a map. Not of places, but of attention. Where the shadows fall. Where the patterns hide. Where a boy might leave a mark, thinking no one would ever see.

The key is not broken. It is rusty. And we have just found the first, faint speck of oil.

The walls are leaning in. They are not just waiting to tell their story. They are starting to show me where the words are hidden. And I, the blank parchment, am learning how to read.



---

# Chapter 5

## CHAPTER 5

The dough is not bread. That is the first wrong thing. Dough should be a promise. A yielding, living cloud that carries the scent of tomorrow’s warmth in its folds. This dough is a sullen lump. It sucks the heat from my hands. It is remembering the oven that is not here. I am kneading absence. The kitchen is a cave of sleeping fires, the great hearth a maw full of grey ash. Only our one candle lives, and the weak, predawn bruise at the high window.

Marta works in silence. Her hands are swift, certain. They do not question the flour, the water, the sour starter. They are treaties with the physical world. My own hands are clumsy translators. I push the heel of my palm into the mass, fold, turn. Push, fold, turn. The rhythm is supposed to be a meditation. It is an accusation. Each push says: *you are here, in a body, doing a thing*. Each fold replies: *but whose body?*

“You’re thinking with your hands,” she says, not looking up from shaping a loaf. “Stop it. Let your hands be hands. The thinking happens elsewhere.”

“Where?” I ask. The word is too loud in the pre-dawn hush.

She flicks a glance at me. “In the cracks. Between the pushes. Not in the push itself.”

I try. I make my mind a blank stone, like the undercroft stone that should be cold. I push, fold, turn. The dough begins to change. Not soften, but to accept. It loses its sullenness. It becomes a thing being worked, not a thing resisting. A shared labour. My skin knows this motion. Some ghost in the muscle knows it. Is it my memory, or his? The line is a thread of cobweb, broken by a passing hand.

This is the preparation. Not of bread, but of me. We are approaching the stronghold. The core conflict is not out there, in the woods or the town. It is in here, in the architecture of a remembered life I am supposed to inhabit. The kitchen, the hall, the staircase with its seven nicks—these are the outer walls. Today, we breach the inner keep. The library. Prior Valens has given his brittle permission. Geoffrey’s lips were a thin, sour line when he told me. A contained victory. A tiny one. But it is solid. It is a fact.

“He will watch you,” Marta says, as if reading the track of my thoughts on my flour-dusted skin. “Geoffrey. He thinks you’ll smudge the vellum with dreaming fingers.”

“What will I find?” I ask her.

She shrugs, a movement that carries the weight of years in this house. “Books. Dust. The quiet that sits in rooms men visit but do not live in. Whether you find *him* there…” She lets the sentence dissolve, like sugar in cold water.

The dough is done. We set the loaves by the residual warmth of the hearth’s core, covered in cloth. They will rise. They believe in a future. I envy them.

*   *   *

The group gathers in the hall as the true light, thin and sharp, cuts through the eastern windows. It is not a team. It is a tense constellation. Prior Valens is the cold, fixed star. Geoffrey is his orbiting moon, grey and pitted with disapproval. Brother Anselm hovers at the periphery, a faint, nervous comet. I am the foreign particle, the dust mote caught in their gravity.

“The library is a place of contemplation, not conjecture,” Prior Valens says. His voice is dry as the pages we will disturb. “You may look. You may touch nothing without Geoffrey’s express oversight. You seek an echo, Silas. Remember: an echo is not a voice. It is a reflection of a sound that is already gone.”

He says my name. It is a key turned in a lock. It does not fit smoothly. I am Silas. I am building the cold from memory. I am learning to knead. I nod.

Geoffrey holds the ring of keys. They are iron, black, unadorned. Not like the tiny, rusted key around my neck. His keys are for locking away. Mine is for… what? A lock we haven’t found? A mechanism that may not exist?

“The air is kept a certain way,” Geoffrey says. His eyes are on my hands. “The books are fragile. Your… method… is not.”

“I will be an archaeologist,” I say, the word from last night feeling strange on my tongue. “Not a storm.”

He sniffs, a sound that dismisses the word and the sentiment. “Follow.”

This is the breach. The walking. The hall’s correct cold is underfoot. Then a passage, narrower, darker. Then a staircase, spiral, of older stone. Not the servant’s stair. This is the spine of the house, twisting up into its private mind. The steps are worn in the centre, hollowed by generations of solitary ascents. I do not look for nicks here. This is not a boy’s territory. This is the realm of men who believe they know things.

The door is oak, banded with iron. Geoffrey’s key grates. The lock thunks with a sound of finality undone. He pushes it open.

The smell is not dust. That is the second wrong thing. It is the smell of time pressed flat. Of slow, cellulose decay. Of leather and glue and the ghost of oak gall ink. It is a sweet, profound rot, different from the apple-sweetness of the undercroft. This is the rot of thought, of stored attention. It is not unpleasant. It is hungry. It pulls the moisture from my throat.

The library is a crypt for living words. Light slants from high, narrow windows, cutting the gloom into slabs of gold and shadow. The books are not just on shelves; they *are* the walls. Leather spines, cracked and peeling, vellum tags, dark wood. A gallery runs around the room, accessed by a slender, wrought-iron staircase. The silence here is different. It is a held breath. A listening silence.

Geoffrey stations himself just inside the door, a sentinel. “You have until Sext. The Prior is generous.”

Brother Anselm flutters to a reading desk, a vast, scarred thing of age-blackened oak. “I have… I have my work. I am here if you… if you need.”

I am inside the stronghold. The outer defences—the kitchen, the hall, the stairs—are passed. This is the dangerous territory. The air itself is a peril. It is thick with the memory-identity of a hundred dead men, a thousand dead thoughts. Where do I begin? A boy’s mark would not be here. Not openly.

I walk the perimeter. My footsteps are swallowed by the silence. I run my eyes over the spines. Theology. Natural philosophy. Liturgy. Chronicles. No titles call to me. No hidden voice whispers from the shelves.

I move to the centre of the room, to the reading desk. A massive folio is open there, held down by velvet-covered weights. It is a bestiary. The page shows a wolf, drawn with clumsy sincerity, its mouth open in a snarl or a song. The text is a dense, black thicket of Latin. My eyes slide over it. They do not know this language. But his might have.

I look up. The gallery. A boy might seek the highest place. The most hidden corner.

“The gallery is unstable,” Geoffrey says from his post. “It is not for browsing.”

“I’m not browsing,” I say, and the sound of my own voice is a violation. “I’m following a thread.”

“There are no threads. Only books.”

I ignore him. I approach the iron staircase. It is delicate, a black lace against the stone. It shudders under my first step, a fine, metallic tremor. Geoffrey makes a sound in his throat. I climb.

The air is warmer up here, closer to the stone ceiling. The light is dimmer. The books here are smaller, more varied. Bundles of letters tied with ribbon. Sheaves of music. Ledgers. The dust is a grey velvet.

I move slowly. I am not looking for a title. I am looking for a disturbance. A gap in the pattern. A place where the dust is less. A book that does not belong.

Halfway along the western wall, I see it. A space. Not a gap where a book has been removed, but a shallow alcove, hidden between two larger, protruding folios. In the alcove, lying flat, is a small, unmarked volume. Its binding is not leather, but wooden boards, warped with age and damp. It is the colour of mud.

My pulse is a thick thing in my neck. I glance down. Geoffrey is watching Brother Anselm pretend to read. Anselm’s fingers are trembling.

I reach into the alcove. My fingertips brush the wood. It is not cold. It is the temperature of the air. Of the room. Of a thing that has been waiting. I slide it out. It is light. Too light. A shell.

I do not open it. Not here. Under Geoffrey’s gaze, it would become a mere object. A curiosity. The violation of a rule. This needs a different kind of attention. The attention of the cracks. Between the pushes.

I tuck the flat volume inside my tunic, against my ribs. It feels like stealing a breath. I take a different, larger book from the shelf at random—a heavy volume of sermons—and carry it with me as I descend the shuddering stairs.

Geoffrey’s eyes are on the book in my hands. He nods, once, a grim satisfaction. He thinks I have chosen a prop. He thinks I am performing expected curiosity. He is right, and he is profoundly wrong.

“Found something worthy?” he asks, the sarcasm thin.

“Words,” I say. “Many words.”

“See that they remain in their proper order.”

*   *   *

I take the book of sermons to a corner. I sit. I open it. The words are a river of black ants, marching in perfect, meaningless lines. I hold my body still. I let my eyes move across the page. I am a blank parchment. But under my tunic, against my skin, the wooden board is a secret. A shared secret. Between him, and me, and the house.

The hour drags. The light in the slabs shifts, climbs the opposite wall. Prior Valens’s allotted time bleeds away. When the bell for Sext begins to toll, distant and solemn, Geoffrey stirs.

“Time.”

I close the heavy book. The sound is a soft, final cough. I return it to its shelf on the ground floor, under his watchful eye. Brother Anselm scurries to close the folio on the reading desk, carefully removing the velvet weights.

We file out. Geoffrey locks the door. The thunk is a period. A full stop.

But I have a comma, hidden against my heart.

*   *

The tension finds its outlet in the refectory, over bread—my bread, from the dough that was not bread. It has risen. It has baked. It is good. It is crust and air and a faint tang of sourness. A fact.

We eat in silence, the four of us. The chewing is the only conversation. Then Prior Valens sets his knife down. The sound is precise.

“Well? Did the library offer any… illumination?”

He asks me, but he looks at Geoffrey.

Geoffrey answers. “He observed. He handled one volume, with care. He was quiet. The room is undisturbed.”

Prior Valens’s gaze slides to me. “And your interior landscape? Any… resonances?”

I swallow a piece of bread. It is suddenly dry. “The silence there is different. It has a shape. It’s a shaped silence.”

He considers this. “Indeed. It is the shape of ordered knowledge. Did you fit within that shape?”

“I was a visitor,” I say. “A guest in the shape.”

A faint frown touches his brow. He dislikes metaphor. He prefers direct report. “Brother Anselm? Did you observe anything of note?”

Anselm jumps as if scalded. “Note? Oh, no. Nothing of *note*. A… a concentration. A reverence for the space. It was… appropriate.”

The interrogation is a slow, cold pressure. They are feeling the edges of my experience, testing for cracks. They fear the memory-identity is not seeping in, but bubbling out. They fear the vessel is not filling, but fermenting.

Geoffrey cannot help himself. “He spent much time in the gallery. Despite the instability.”

“The gallery,” Prior Valens echoes. “A place of overview. Of seeing the whole room. Were you seeking perspective, Silas?”

“I was seeking air,” I say, which is both true and a lie. “The air below is… pressed flat.”

“And was the gallery’s air more to your liking?”

“It was older,” I say. “It tasted of the rafters.”

My answers are evasive. They are creating a tension within the group. Prior Valens’s doubt is a scholarly scepticism. Geoffrey’s is a custodial disgust. Anselm’s is a form of terror. They are not aligned, but their disquiet forms a net around me.

“The work is slow,” Prior Valens says, finally. “It is not a matter of finding a single clue, like a child’s treasure hunt. It is an osmotic process. You must allow the saturation to occur.”

“I am kneading,” I say, without thinking.

The word lands in the silence. All three men look at me.

“Kneading?” Prior Valens asks.

“The dough. This morning. With Marta. You push, and fold, and turn. It is not fast. It is a transformation of the material. By steady work.” I am speaking too quickly. “This feels the same. I am the dough. The house is the hands. You are the hands. I am being kneaded.”

Geoffrey’s lip curls. “A quaint analogy.”

But Prior Valens is watching me with a new, sharp interest. “Kneading requires a knowledge of the desired outcome. The loaf. Do you have a sense of the loaf, Silas?”

The question is a needle. It goes deep. Do I have a sense of the finished man I am to become? The memory-identity, fully risen?

“No,” I say, and it is the truest word I have spoken today. “I only know the feeling of being worked.”

*   *

The plan is finalized at dusk. It is based on flawed, incomplete information. My information. I have the hidden book. They have my report of shaped silences and aerial tastes. The plan is simply to continue. To return me to the environments of his life. The garden tomorrow. The scriptorium the day after. A gentle, relentless pressure. The osmotic process.

They believe they are in control of the experiment. They believe the library yielded nothing but metaphor. They are wrong. I have breached their outer defence—their watchful curation of my experience—and taken a piece of the core.

In my cell, with the door barred by a simple chair, I take it out.

The wooden boards are rough. There is no clasp, no lock. Just a flap of leather that serves as a hinge. I open it.

The pages are not vellum. They are scraps. Different kinds of paper. A thicker, rougher sheet. A fine, almost translucent leaf from somewhere else. A piece of parchment that is darker, greasier. They are cut unevenly, stitched together with crude, waxed thread.

This is not a book. It is a secret.

On the first page, drawn in brown ink that has faded to the colour of weak tea, is a map. Not of the house, or the grounds. It is a map of a room. *This* room. My cell. But it is wrong. The bed is in the wrong place. The window is larger. There is a shelf on the wall that does not exist. And in the corner, where my empty washstand sits, the map shows a small, careful ‘X’.

On the next page, a list. Not of sins, or loves. Of sounds.
*Scuff of Brother Anselm’s left foot (the sole is thin).*
*Prior’s key-jangle (two full revolutions before the lock).*
*Marta’s spoon against the copper pot (three beats, then a rest).*
*The wind in the east chimney (it sings a B-flat, ask Brother Cillian).*

Brother Cillian. A name not yet spoken.

Another page. A drawing of a hawk, mid-flight, wings stretched. The lines are swift, sure. They capture not just the shape, but the tension in the shape. The potential energy. Underneath, in a cramped, angular hand: *He understands the air. He is not subject to it.*

Page after page. Observations. Sketches of leaves, of beetles, of the profile of a man I do not recognize—a strong nose, a heavy brow. Lists of Latin verbs conjugated incorrectly, with angry corrections in a different ink. A recipe for something called “Lamplack” involving soot and wine and egg white. A pressed flower, brown and fragile as a ghost’s breath.

This is not a diary. It is a field guide. A manual of attention. It is the map of attention I had begun to sense last night, made manifest. He was an archaeologist of his own life. Digging up the details, cataloguing the hidden patterns. He was building a memory-identity from the outside in, from the specific to the whole. Not waiting for it to be poured into him. He was constructing it, piece by observed piece.

And I am doing the same.

The shattering is not loud. It is a quiet, interior collapse. A fundamental shift through the lens of memory-identity.

I have been thinking of it as a possession. A haunting. A filling of a vessel. They have too. The Prior with his saturation, Geoffrey with his contamination, Anselm with his terror.

But this book—*his* book—tells a different story. Memory-identity is not a liquid to be absorbed. It is a structure to be built. A collaboration between the clues of a life and the labour of attention. He was building Silas. And now, I am building Silas. We are not the same builder, but we are using the same plans. The same hidden notations.

I am not a blank parchment being written upon.
I am a scribe, copying from a fragmented text.
And I have just found the source manuscript.

The hook is not in the final page. It is on the inside of the back wooden board. Scratched into the grain, almost invisible, are two words. Not in Latin. In our common tongue. A private, desperate plea, or a statement of fact. The rusted key around my neck seems to grow warm, a sympathetic vibration.

The words are:

*REMEMBER THE COLD.*



---

# Chapter 6

## CHAPTER 6

The stone is not cold. That is the first wrong thing. The stone of the undercroft should drink the warmth from your back, should be a slab of winter pulled up from the earth’s gut. This stone is a lukewarm tooth. My shoulder grinds against it, the wool of my tunic catching on the grit. I am remembering the cold that is not here. I am building the cold from memory. The damp, the seeping, the way it would climb your spine like a slow vine. That is how it should be. Not this dry, breathless cling. The air tastes of dust and old incense, but underneath, a sweetness rots. Overripe apples. Forgotten in a corner. Dying into wine.

Footsteps above. Not the scuff of Brother Anselm’s hesitant shuffle, nor the confident slap of the Prior’s soles. These are a stranger’s feet. Measured. Patient. They circle the chapel floor directly over my head, a slow, predatory orbit. I press my spine harder into the stone, willing it to absorb me. The book is a live coal against my ribs, bound in leather and hidden beneath my tunic. *Remember the cold.* The words are a splinter in my mind, working deeper with every heartbeat.

The footsteps stop. A grating sound, stone on stone. The trapdoor.

Light, thick and yellow as old broth, spills down the ladder. A silhouette fills the square of it. Not Geoffrey’s wiry tension, not the Prior’s solid block. This shape is different. Taller. Thinner. It descends with an economical grace, each rung accepted without a sound. He steps onto the earth floor and the light from above crowns him, but his face is in shadow. He brings his own silence with him, a pocket of dead air.

“Silas.”

The voice is dry. A page turning in an empty library. It is not a question.

I say nothing. My own name, in this mouth, is a trap.

He steps forward, into the weak glow of my single tallow candle. He is perhaps fifty, perhaps seventy; his face has the quality of vellum, finely wrinkled but taut over sharp bones. His eyes are the colour of a winter sky just before snow. He wears the black robe of a Benedictine, but it is too clean, too perfectly draped. There is no fray at the cuffs, no stain of ink or earth. It is a costume.

“I am Brother Alaric. Visitor-General.” He smiles. It does not touch his eyes. “You have been much discussed.”

He begins to walk a slow circle around the edge of the undercroft, his fingers trailing along the shelves where the monastery’s oldest, least-used texts are stored. He is not looking at them. He is measuring the space. Measuring me.

“A curious case,” he continues, his voice a low hum. “A vessel, found empty on the shore. Filled, by degrees, with a foreign tide. The Prior believes in saturation. A gradual, gentle process. Brother Geoffrey fears contamination. A sickness to be purged.” He stops, facing me. “I am interested in architecture.”

My blood becomes a slurry of ice-chips. *Architecture.*

“What is being built here, Silas? And upon whose foundation?”

He knows about the book. He must. The thought is a rat’s tooth, gnawing. He has not asked for it. He is waiting for me to betray its presence with a glance, a protective hand.

“I remember nothing of my own,” I say, the practiced line ash on my tongue.

“Ah, but you do.” He takes another step closer. The sweet-rot smell is stronger now, and I realize it is on him. Not apples. Something else. Myrrh, maybe, gone cloying and foul. “You remember the cold. You remember the grit of this stone. You remember the weight of a key around your neck. These are not your memories. They are his. And yet you hold them. You have arranged them. You are, day by day, constructing a facsimile of a dead man inside your living skull. That is architecture.”

He has named it. Made it a thing of beams and joints and load-bearing walls. My project. My crime.

“It is not a possession,” I whisper.

“No,” he agrees, and his winter-eyes gleam. “It is far more interesting. Possession is passive. This is active. A deliberate, meticulous reconstruction. You are not haunted. You are a scholar. A copyist. The most ambitious copyist I have ever encountered.” He tilts his head. “Where did you find the original text?”

The question hangs in the dusty air. A direct thrust. I feel the shape of the book against my skin, its corners pressing like accusing fingers.

“I don’t know what you mean.”

“The Prior is a gardener. He wishes to nurture a familiar plant in new soil. Geoffrey is a physician. He wishes to cure a fever. I,” he says, spreading his hands, “am a librarian. I wish to see the source material.”

He moves so fast it is not a movement but a change in state. One moment he is three paces away, the next his hand is at my throat, not squeezing, but resting. His fingers are cold. Colder than the stone ever was. A calculated, artificial cold.

“The key,” he says. “Where is it?”

I cannot speak. I will not. I am building a wall inside my mind, stone by remembered stone. *Remember the cold.* I build it with the memory of the true undercroft chill, the vine up the spine. His touch is a blasphemy against that memory.

His other hand darts inside my tunic. I twist, but he is implacable. His fingers close not on the key, but on the book. He draws it forth, and the leather seems to shriek in the silence. He releases my throat, steps back, cradling the volume as if it were a holy relic. A sickly triumph lights his face.

“Ah,” he breathes. “The foundational text.”

He does not open it. He simply holds it. My map. My plans. The compiled attention of the man I am supposed to be. I feel eviscerated. Hollowed out. The structure I have been so carefully building inside me groans, its beams sagging.

“You have done well, Silas,” he says, his voice almost kind. “You have gathered the scattered fragments. You have begun the work of reassembly. But you lack context. You lack the *schema*. You are trying to rebuild a cathedral from a pile of carved stones, without knowing the blueprint. That,” he says, tapping the book, “is merely a list of stones.”

He turns and walks toward the ladder. My legs unlock. I stumble after him, a wordless cry caught in my chest.

“Wait.”

He pauses, one foot on the bottom rung. He looks back, not at me, but at the space I occupy. As if I am already fading.

“What will you do?”

“I will complete your education,” he says. “I will provide the blueprint. And then, Silas, we will see what we have built. We will see if it can bear the weight.”

He climbs. The light swallows him. The trapdoor grates shut, and the bolt, a heavy, final sound, slides home.

I am in the dark. The true dark, now that my candle has guttered out in the disturbance. The book is gone. The physical proof of my theory, my fragile understanding, is gone. I am not a scribe. I am a blank parchment again. The cold I have been remembering is now a real, present thing. It leeches up from the earth, it fingers my skin. Alaric did not take the key, but it is useless. It unlocks nothing in this room.

This is the defeat. Not a battle, but a quiet, professional confiscation. My mind is the site of the defeat. The architecture of my new self—the Silas built from observation and stolen memory—has been declared unsound. Condemned. The foreman has taken the plans.

I slide down the wall until I am sitting on the earth. The cold seeps into my bones. I let it. I invite it. *Remember the cold.* It is all I have left. The memory and the reality, merging.

Hours pass. Or days. Time is a slurry in the dark. I do not sleep. I deconstruct. I take apart the identity I had been building, beam by beam, stone by stone. The careful observations, the patterns in the rain, the taste of the broth, the shape of Geoffrey’s anger. Without the book to anchor them, they are just sensations. Floating signifiers. I am not a scribe copying a text. I am a blank page, and the ink has been spilled, leaving only chaotic, meaningless blots.

This is what Alaric intended. This is the demolition before the new construction.

I think of the man in the book. The one who sketched leaves and beetles and conjugated Latin verbs wrong. He built a self to survive. He built a Silas. And I, finding his tools, tried to do the same. But I was building on sand. On someone else’s memories. I was building a ghost a house to live in, and now the ghost has evicted me.

The despair is not hot. It is this cold. It is absolute. It is the understanding that I have no foundation. No bedrock of my own. I am a echo in a shell, and the sound that made me has ceased.

In that absolute zero, a revelation occurs. It does not come as a light. It comes as a deeper shade of dark.

He left me the key.

He took the book, the precious blueprint, but he left the rusted iron key around my neck. He dismissed it. To him, it is just a relic. A part of the costume. But it was important enough to scratch a reminder of on the book’s board. *Remember the cold.* The key is part of that memory. It is not a symbol. It is a tool.

And I have been thinking like a scribe. Like a builder. Copying, assembling.

What if I am not a scribe?

What if I am a locksmith?

The thought is a tiny, hard seed in the frozen waste of my mind. It has no heat, but it has density. It has potential.

Alaric sees architecture. A structure to be analyzed, copied, controlled. He wants the blueprint to understand the building. But a key does not care about blueprints. A key understands only the lock. The hidden, internal geometry of obstruction.

My memory-identity is not a building. It is a lock. And the memories—his memories, my observations, the cold, the grit, the taste of broth—are not stones. They are the wards and pins inside that lock. I have been trying to build a house out of lock-parts. It will never stand. But arrange them correctly, feel their subtle alignment… and something might turn. Something might open.

I do not need to *be* Silas. I need to *unlock* him.

The sacrifice is immediate. I must sacrifice the goal. The desire to be whole, to be coherent, to have a name and a history that makes sense. I must embrace the fragments. Not as construction materials, but as tactile clues. I must stop trying to read the text, and start feeling the impression the pen left on the page.

I stand. My joints protest, stiff with cold and stillness. I take the key from around my neck. The iron is icy. I close my fist around it until its teeth bite into my palm. The pain is a point of focus.

I begin to move through the blackness of the undercroft. I am not looking for anything. I am feeling. My free hand trails along the shelves, over the spines of forgotten books, through the dust on the stone. I am not Silas the amnesiac, or Silas the copyist. I am a blind man in a strange room, learning its shape by touch.

My foot connects with something solid, not stone. A small, heavy object. It skitters away with a wooden scrape. I kneel, patting the earth floor. My fingers find it. A box. Small, plain, unvarnished wood. I had not noticed it before. It was hidden in the dark, under the lowest shelf.

I bring it to where the trapdoor is, where a faint, grey seam of light from the world above outlines its shape. I can see just enough. There is no lock on the box. Only a simple latch. I lift it.

Inside, resting on a bed of faded velvet, is a knife. A seax. A single-edged blade, about the length of my hand from wrist to fingertip. The metal is dark, stained. The handle is worn smooth, bound with leather. It is a tool, not a weapon. Or a weapon that has been used as a tool.

Beneath it, folded small, is a piece of parchment. I unfold it. The writing is the same as in the book. Hurried, angular.

*They are digging in the orchard. They seek the root of the contamination. They will find only bones. The lock is not in the earth. It is in the air. It is in the remembering. The key is not for a door. It is for a season. Remember the COLD.*

*C-O-L-D.*

The final word is not written. It is spelled out, letter by letter, as if for a child. Or for someone who might need to learn it anew.

C-O-L-D.

Not an instruction. An acronym. A code.

My mind, thinking in locks now, not walls, tries the tumblers. C-O-L-D. The letters mean nothing. Then I think of the lists in the book. The conjugated verbs. The recipes. The man was a cataloguer. A labeller.

*Custodia Oblitus, Lumen Deletum.*

The Latin comes from nowhere and everywhere. It floats up from the deep, cold well of the memory that is not mine. It is rough, probably incorrect. But it feels true.

*Guardian Forgotten, Light Erased.*

It is not about the weather. It is about a person. A role. *Custodia.* A keeper. A guardian. Of what? Forgotten. *Oblitus.* And a light, erased. *Lumen Deletum.*

The seax in the box is heavy. Real. It is the first object I have encountered that belongs purely to the physical world of *before*, untouched by the Prior’s theories or Geoffrey’s fears or Alaric’s architectures. It is a fact. It was hidden here, by him, for me. For this moment.

The key in my hand, the knife in the box, the code on the parchment. These are not pieces of a self. They are pieces of a lock. And I am holding all of them.

Above me, the bolt on the trapdoor shrieks as it is drawn back.

Light floods down. Not the gentle yellow of before, but the harsh, white light of many lanterns. Figures descend. Brother Anselm, his face a mask of terrified apology. Two burly lay brothers I have seen at the mill. And behind them, Alaric.

He sees the open box in my hands. He sees the seax. His winter-eyes narrow, not with anger, but with recalculated interest. He thought he had taken all the pieces off the board. He missed one.

“Take him,” he says, his dry voice cutting the air. “The orchard. It is time to see what the digging has unearthed.”

The lay brothers seize my arms. The key is still clenched in my fist. The seax is wrenched from the box and taken. But the parchment I had already, instinctively, crumpled into my palm, a tiny ball of hidden text.

As they manhandle me up the ladder, through the chapel, and out into the blinding grey of the pre-dawn, I am not resisting. I am thinking.

*Custodia Oblitus.* The forgotten guardian.
*Lumen Deletum.* The erased light.

They are digging in the orchard for bones. For the root.

But the root is not a body. The lock is in the air. It is in the remembering.

And I am no longer a scribe, or a builder, or a vessel.

I am a key, turning in a frozen lock.

And deep in the orchard, under the gnarled, sleeping trees, something is about to be opened.



---

# Chapter 7

## CHAPTER 7

The orchard is not sleeping. That is the second wrong thing. The trees are a congregation of black bones, their limbs twisted in a permanent, silent scream against the grey wash of dawn. They do not sleep. They watch. The air here is different from the undercroft’s dry cling; it is a wet cloth pressed to the face, smelling of turned earth and the iron tang of something older than soil. The sweetness of rot is here too, but it is not from apples. It is a deeper fermentation. The ground itself, exhaling.

Alaric walks ahead, a grey spectre between the black trunks. The two lay brothers, their hands like root-knots on my arms, propel me forward. My boots sink into the churned mud. They have been digging. Not in one place, but in many. The orchard floor is a body pocked with fresh, dark wounds.

“Here,” Alaric says, stopping before the largest excavation. It is a gash perhaps ten feet long, four feet wide. The raw earth at its edges is piled high, a levee of darkness. At the bottom, maybe five feet down, the diggers have struck not more soil, but a sheet of fitted stone. A ledger slab. The kind that seals a tomb.

Brother Anselm hovers at the pit’s rim, wringing his hands. His eyes dart from the hole to me to Alaric’s back. He is a leaf caught in a downdraft.

“You sought a root,” Alaric says, not turning. His voice is a dry scrape, a tool on stone. “A source. A first cause for the silence that plagues this place. The Prior sought it in prayer. Geoffrey sought it in fear. I sought it in structure, in the bones of this house. We were all digging in the wrong medium.”

He finally turns. His face is pale in the flat light, his eyes the colour of a frozen stream. He looks at me as if I am a newly discovered crack in a foundation wall.

“The root is not a metaphor. It is a fact. It lies here. And you…” He gestures to the lay brothers. They release me, but do not retreat. “You are the spade that finally struck it. Your remembering. Your… agitation. It has stirred the earth. Made the ground soft. Ready to give up its secret.”

I say nothing. The crumpled parchment is a hot coal in my clenched fist. The key, taken from me in the chapel, is gone. The seax is in the belt of one of the lay brothers. I have only the words in my head, and the ball of hidden text in my hand.

*Custodia Oblitus. Lumen Deletum.*

“Break the seal,” Alaric commands.

The lay brothers jump down into the pit, their boots thudding dully on the stone. They carry iron bars, wedges. They set to work at the edge of the slab, where mortar has held for centuries. The sound is a violation—a grinding, cracking complaint that echoes through the silent trees. Anselm flinches with each blow.

I am not looking at the pit. I am looking at the orchard. The pattern of the trees. The way the paths between them are not random, but channels, like the negative space between the lines of a drawn labyrinth. The memory is not mine. It is older. It is the memory of the land. This was not always an orchard. The trees were planted in rows to obscure the lines beneath, to sweeten the air above. To guard.

*Custodia.*

The stone slab cracks with a sound like a giant knuckle popping. One end tilts upward. A sigh escapes from the darkness below—not the smell of decay I expected, but a breath of cold, sterile air, like that from a deep cellar. It carries a hint of ozone, of a snuffed candle.

The lay brothers strain, muscles corded. With a final, grating shriek, the slab is pushed aside, revealing a black maw.

Alaric takes a lantern from Anselm, who will not meet his eye. He holds it over the opening. The light does not flood in; it is swallowed, then reluctantly shows a set of steep stone steps descending into a darkness thicker than the night.

“Bring him,” Alaric says.

They pull me forward. I am made to go first.

***

The steps are not hewn from the local stone. They are a grey, granular rock, imported, fitted with a precision that speaks of a purpose beyond a root-cellar. Each step is shallow, forcing a slow, deliberate descent. The air grows colder with each one, a cold that is familiar. It is the cold I tried to remember in the undercroft. The true cold. It climbs my spine like a slow vine.

The lantern light behind me throws my own distorted shadow down into the void. The chamber at the bottom is small, perhaps twelve feet square. The walls are the same fitted stone. It is not a tomb. There are no bones, no sarcophagus.

In the centre of the room is a plinth. On the plinth rests a single object.

It is a box. But not a box of wood or iron. It is made of a dull, non-reflective stone, dark as basalt. It is about the size of a large book, its surfaces perfectly smooth, seamless. There is no keyhole, no hinge, no latch. It is less a container and more a solid idea of containment.

Alaric pushes past me, his breath frosting in the lantern light. He circles the plinth, his eyes hungry. He reaches out a hand, but stops just short of touching the stone box. A superstitious hesitation, quickly masked.

“See?” he whispers. The chamber drinks his voice, leaving no echo. “The root. The source of the silence. Not a person. A thing. A *device*.”

I step closer. The cold is emanating from the box. It is not the cold of absence, but of a profound, focused stillness. Looking at it, the memory of sound—of the scribe’s quill scratch, of the chant in the chapel, of Geoffrey’s frantic whispers—feels like a distant, foolish dream.

“The *Custodia Oblitus*,” I say. The words feel at home in this air.

Alaric’s head snaps up. “What did you say?”

“The forgotten guardian.” I look from the box to him. “That is what was written. In the undercroft. *Custodia Oblitus*. And *Lumen Deletum*. The erased light.”

His winter-eyes glitter. He had the key, the seax. But he did not have the parchment. He did not have the words. For the first time, I have something he does not.

“A guardian,” he murmurs, circling again. “Yes. It guards silence. It enforces it. The stories… the whispers of a ‘quiet place’ at the heart of the abbey, a place where God’s voice could be heard most clearly. Not a metaphor. A literal quiet. A zone of perfect auditory stillness, generated by this.” He gestures at the box. “A tool for contemplation. But tools can be turned. Amplified. What if the ‘erased light’ is not a spiritual concept? What if it is the erasure of *all* stimulus? Sound, then light, then… thought itself? A perfect void.”

He is building his architecture again, his theory. He sees a machine. A broken tool to be fixed, understood, controlled.

I see something else. I see the final lock. The one the key, the seax, and the code were meant to address. The lock is not on the box. The box *is* the lock. And it is locked onto the world.

“How do you open it?” he demands, turning to me. “The words. What else did they say?”

I unclench my fist slightly, feeling the parchment ball. “They were instructions. A sequence. For the guardian.”

“Give them to me.”

“They are not for you.”

The lay brothers shift behind me. Alaric’s face hardens. “You are a vessel. A means. You have served your purpose. The memory is mine now. The key is mine. The object is mine. The words will be mine.”

He nods to the brothers. They grab me again. One pins my arms while the other pries open my fingers. The tiny ball of parchment falls to the stone floor. Alaric swoops, picks it up, carefully unfolds it. He holds it close to the lantern, his lips moving silently as he deciphers the cramped, inked Latin.

His eyes widen. Then narrow in confusion. “This is… nonsense. A list. *Primus: Coram Custodia. Secundus: Ferrum Oblationis. Tertius: Verbum Clavis.* Before the guardian. The iron offering. The word-key.” He looks at the seax at the lay brother’s belt, then at the stone box. “The iron offering. The seax. It is not a weapon. It is a component. A sacrifice to the mechanism.” A fierce, cold excitement grips him. “The key was not for a physical lock. It was to bring us to the place of the offering. And the word-key… the final command. It must be spoken here, before the guardian, after the offering is made.”

He takes the seax from the lay brother. He holds the heavy, notched blade, looking from it to the seamless stone box. There is no slot, no aperture.

“*Coram Custodia*,” he mutters. Before the guardian. He steps directly in front of the plinth, facing the box. He raises the seax, point downward, in both hands, like a priest with a ritual blade.
