The moan is all around me, in the walls, in the air I breathe. The stairs go down and down. I have passed the level of the crypts. I am below the bones of saints. I am in the earth’s throat.

I lose count of steps. Time becomes the rhythm of my descent. The air grows warmer, drier. The lightning-struck smell is stronger. It is the smell of a power that is not divine. It is elemental. A memory of fire and pressure.

The stairs end.

I stand on a rough, uneven floor. A cavern. I can feel the vastness of it in the echo of my breath, in the way the moan hangs in the space. Somewhere, water drips. A slow, maddening cadence.

And then, a light.

A faint, sourceless, greenish phosphorescence. It swims in the air, clinging to patches of lichen on the walls. It reveals the shape of the cavern. It is not natural. The walls are squared. The marks of tools are everywhere—not the clean chisels of masons, but the brutal, hacking blows of picks. This is a mine. An old, forgotten mine. The abbey was built atop a mine.

In the center of the cavern, the source of the moan.

It is a pillar. A single, massive column of raw, uncut stone, rising from the floor to vanish into the darkness of the ceiling high above. It is the root of the mountain. The foundation stone of the foundation.

And it is cracked.

A fissure splits it from floor to a height twice my own. It is not a clean crack. It is a jagged, weeping wound. From within the crack, the greenish light pulses. With each pulse, the moan swells. The grinding is the sound of the pillar’s insides, moving against themselves. The memory Hask triggered is here. In the stone’s own recollection of being cut, being shaped, being *used*.

This is the mechanism. The cup was the trigger, but the bell is the mountain. And the mountain is remembering it is a quarry.

At the base of the pillar, two shapes.

Hask lies on his side. He is still. The cup is beside him, rolled a few inches away. It gleams dully in the phantom light. It looks inert. A dead thing.

The other shape is older.

It is a skeleton. Robes long since turned to dust-laden threads. It is propped against the pillar, in a posture of repose. In its lap lies a harp. A small, travel-worn harp. The wood is petrified. The strings are gone, save for one. A single, silver wire, stretched taut.

The singer.

He did not die in the sun. He came down here. He came to the source of the wrongness. He brought his unfinished song to the place where the memory of making lived. And he poured it into the cup. Not to fill it. To *bind* it. To use the song’s tension as a suture on the crack in the world.

He failed. The cup was not a vessel for healing. It was a lens. It focused the song’s power, but on the wrong thing. On the memory, not the fracture. It started the unraveling he meant to stop.

I walk forward. The air hums with power. My skin prickles. I kneel beside Hask. His eyes are open. They see nothing. His breath is shallow. He is empty. The cup has drunk him. His mind is a dry bed. He is a shell holding the echo of a river.

I look at the cup. I do not touch it.

I look at the singer. At his harp. The single silver string.

The moan pulses. A larger piece of stone scales off the pillar with a sound like breaking pottery. The crack widens. The green light flares.

The singer’s answer was wrong. He tried to use a song of making to heal a wound of making. He poured memory into a cup that craved memory.

My answer is smaller. It is not about memory. It is about now. It is about a thing that rings true, and then knows how to stop.

I look at my dented silver bowl.

Then I look at the singer’s last string.

The hook is gone from my chest. But a new understanding is there. A terrible, simple understanding.

The cup wants a story. A finished story.

So I will give it one.

I reach out. I do not touch the cup. I touch the harp. My fingers brush the petrified wood. It is cold as death. I find the peg holding the single silver string. It is stiff. Frozen with age.

I take the silver bowl. I place it on the stone floor, before the skeletal hands of the singer.

I take the loose end of the silver string. My hands are steady. They are a bell-maker’s hands. I tie the string to the rim of the bowl. A clumsy, desperate knot.

I have no bridge. No soundpost. But the pillar is the soundbox. The crack is the soundhole.

I tighten the string. I turn the peg. The string resists. It sings a single, protesting note as it stretches. It is the same note as the hum. The pure tone, but here, in the deep, it is the root of the tone. The mother note.

I tighten it further. The note rises in pitch. It cuts through the moan. The green light in the crack flickers.

I am not making a harp. I am making a monochord. A single-stringed instrument of measurement. Of truth.

I pluck the string.

The sound is clean. It is a lance of silver in the green gloom. It rings in the bowl. The bowl amplifies it, gives it a body, a brief, shining life. The sound echoes in the cavern, off the mined walls, off the weeping pillar.

The moan hesitates.

I pluck it again. And again. A steady, rhythmic pulse. Not a song. A heartbeat. A present, insistent *now*.

*Here. Here. Here.*

Each pluck is a denial of memory. It is a statement of existence. This bowl. This string. This moment. This is not a story of the past. It is a fact of the present.

The cup, lying on its side, begins to vibrate. A faint, sympathetic buzz. It is listening. It is a void that hears a thing that is full, and finished, and *over* with each ring.

I keep plucking. My finger grows sore. The string is cutting. I do not stop. The bowl rings. The sound is not beautiful. It is insistent. It is an argument.

The crack in the pillar pulses in time to my plucking. Slowing. The green light dims. The grinding moan softens, becoming a groan, then a sigh.

The cup’s vibration increases. It rocks on its curved base. It is trying to drink the sound. But the sound is not a story. It is a declaration. It has no past. It has only its own brief life, and its willing death.

I pluck. The bowl rings. The sound fades.

I pluck. It rings. It fades.

*I am. I end. I am. I end.*

The cup cannot consume it. There is nothing to consume but the moment, and the moment is already gone, leaving only the memory of a true ring, which is not a story, but an echo of a fact.

The cup tips over. It lies still. The dull grey metal seems to grow duller. It is just a shape again. A dead lock.

The light in the crack dies. The moan stops.

The silence is absolute. It is the silence after a great noise has ceased. It is thick. It presses on the ears.

Then, a new sound. A soft, settling sound. Like a sigh of relief. From the pillar. From the walls. The mountain is remembering itself again. As a mountain. Not a quarry.

I let go of the string. My finger is bleeding. The silver bowl sits on the stone, the string tied to it, a ridiculous, beautiful thing. It worked. It was the answer.

But in the new silence, I hear it.

A faint, dry rustle.

I look at the singer’s skeleton. The petrified harp in its lap.

The bones are moving. Not much. A settling. A final release.

The skull tilts forward. It looks at me. The empty sockets hold the darkness of the deep earth.

And from the place where a voice should be, a sound emerges. Not a voice. A vibration of air through ancient bone. A whisper made of dust and finality.

*“Thank you.”*

The skeleton collapses into a heap of bones. The harp clatters to the stone, shattering into fragments of fossilized wood. The silver string goes slack.

The story is over. The singer can rest.

I sit in the dark and the quiet. I am trembling. I have given the cup a finished story. I have ended the longest question.

But as I sit there, the hollow in my chest returns. Not a hook. A different emptiness.

I have used my bowl. I have tied it to a dead man’s string and played a truth that broke a curse. It is no longer just an answer. It is a relic. A part of a story that is now finished.

I pick it up. The dent is there. The string is still tied. It is no longer mine. It belongs to this place. To this silence.

I leave it there. I set it gently beside the heap of bones.

I stand. My legs are weak. I look at Hask. He is breathing. He will live. But he will be empty. A man who remembered himself away.

I do not touch the cup. I leave it lying on its side.

I turn and climb the stairs. The spiral is long. The darkness is complete. I climb by feel. I climb toward a world that has stopped shaking. Toward a silence that is not a question.

When I emerge into the chapter house, the grey dawn has broken into a pale, clean sunlight. It slants through the high windows, illuminating the dust motes hanging in the still air. The Prior is gone. The tombstone still lies tilted. The hole is a black eye in the floor.

I walk out into the cloister. The cold air is sweet. The hum is gone. The abbey is silent. A true silence. A silence after a fever has broken.

I go to the bell tower. I climb to where my brother’s great bell hangs. I put my hand on its cold bronze flank. It is silent. It is waiting.

The peace I feel is not the peace of a period. It is the peace of a blank page. After the last sentence of a long, terrible story has been written.

The cup is silenced. The singer is at rest. The mountain is whole.

But I am not.

I have left my answer in the dark. I have nothing to ring.

I am a bell-maker without a bell.

And in the new, clean silence, I realize the hook was not pulling me to the cup. It was pulling me to the making. To the need to fill the silence with something true.

The cup’s question is answered.

But mine is just beginning.

*What do I make now?*

The silence waits. It is not a hungry silence. It is a patient one. It is the silence before the first note.

I look at my empty, bleeding hands.

They will have to learn a new shape.



---

# Chapter 17

## CHAPTER 17

The silence is a new kind of stone. It is not the old, hungry silence of the mountain’s wound. That was a sucking thing. A mouth. This is a floor. A foundation. It is the quiet of a room after the last guest has gone, and you are left with the empty cups and the echoes of their voices. The dust motes hang in the sun from the bell tower window, suspended in the clear, cold light. They do not dance. They are settled. The great bell beside me is a dark sun, a bronze cliff. My hand on its lip is a dry leaf against a frozen lake.

I am a bell-maker without a bell.

The words are true, but they are not the whole truth. I am a man who has un-made a thing. I have taken a story that was a knot and pulled the right thread. It came undone. It lies now in the undercroft, a heap of bones and a dented tin cup and a piece of twine that was my answer. The unraveling was the work. The curse was a song sung wrong. I found the true note. I sang it. The song stopped.

The work is done.

But my hands are still shaped for the work. They curl at my sides, remembering the weight of a hammer, the tension of a tuning fork. They remember the hook in my breastbone, that insistent, physical pull toward a wrongness that needed righting. The hook is gone. In its place is a hollow. A space where a compass needle used to point. It does not hurt. It is simply empty. A door has been shut, and I am on the wrong side of it.

I descend the tower. The steps are just steps. They do not wind into a throat. The air is just air. It does not taste of metal or memory. The cloister walk is a rectangle of pale light and sharp shadow. The frost on the flagstones is ordinary frost. It crunches under my boots with a sound that goes nowhere, means nothing. It is just sound.

I find Hask in the infirmary. He lies on a pallet by the hearth. Brother Anselm is wringing out a cloth in a basin. The old monk’s hands tremble, but his movements are precise. He lays the cloth on Hask’s forehead. Hask’s eyes are open. They look at the smoke-blackened beams of the ceiling. They are not seeing them.

“He breathes,” Anselm says, not turning. “His heart beats. He takes water if you tilt his head. But he does not speak. He does not blink unless the cloth touches his lid.”

I stand at the foot of the pallet. Hask is a man emptied. A vessel poured out. He remembered himself away. He followed his memory into the cup’s song and he did not come back. What is left is the clay. The shape of a man, without the fire inside.

“Will he return?” I ask.

Anselm shakes his head. It is not a gesture of no. It is a gesture of not knowing. “His soul has gone to a place memory cannot map. It is a pilgrimage with no return path. The body is a faithful dog. It waits. It may wait until it starves.”

I look at Hask’s hands. They are crossed on his chest. They are the hands that held a sword, that gripped a rope, that carved his daughter’s name into a piece of ship-wood. They are empty now. They have forgotten their purpose.

“The Prior?” I ask.

“Gone to the village. To tell them it is over. To see what is left.” Anselm finally looks at me. His eyes are red-rimmed, but clear. “He said you broke it. He said the mountain is healed.”

“The wound is closed,” I say.

Anselm nods. He looks back at Hask. “Some wounds close over nothing. They become a different kind of hollow.”

I leave the infirmary. The abbey moves around me with the slow, careful rhythm of convalescence. A novice sweeps the chapter house floor, his broom whispering against the stone. Another carries a bucket of mortar from the yard, his breath a white plume. They are mending the physical world. The tombstone has been righted. The hole in the floor has been filled with rubble and sand, a temporary patch. The real mending will come later. They look at me as I pass. Their glances are not fearful. They are curious. I am the man who went into the dark and came back with silence. I am a curiosity. A story that is already being shaped in their minds. I can feel it. The telling will sand down my edges, make me into a symbol. The bell-maker who silenced the ghost. It is a cleaner story than the truth.

The truth is I followed a hook in my flesh. I did what my hands knew how to do. I solved a puzzle made of grief and stone. And now I am unemployed.

I walk out of the abbey gates. The world outside is shockingly bright. The sky is a hard, pale blue, scrubbed clean. The mountain stands against it, a solid mass. It does not hum. It is just a mountain. A thing of rock and snow and silence. The path down to the village is a scar of churned mud and fractured ice. The earth here remembers the shaking. It will for a season.

I do not go to the village. I turn east, along the goat track that skirts the abbey’s outer wall. The track leads to the higher meadows, now buried under snow, and to the place where the abbey’s dead are buried. A small, walled plot on a lee slope, sheltered from the worst of the wind by a stand of skeletal pines.

The gate is iron, rusted. It whines. The sound is lonely. The graves are humps under the snow, marked with simple stone crosses. Names, dates. A few lines from scripture. The snow has drifted over some, leaving only the tip of the cross visible, like a drowning man’s hand.

I am not here for the named dead.

At the far corner, where the wall meets the cliff face, is a different kind of mound. It is longer. It is fresh. The earth piled there is dark, almost black against the white. It has not yet been covered by snow. This is the grave for the bones from the undercroft. They were carried up this morning, after I emerged. A communal resting place. A pit for forgotten men.

They are not forgotten now. They have a story. They were the singers. Their song is done.

I stand before the raw earth. It smells of cold soil and deep places. There is no marker. Perhaps one will be made. A stone that says: HERE LIE THE FORGOTTEN. REST FINALLY GRANTED.

But they are not the only forgotten thing.

My fingers are cold. I flex them. The cuts on my palms from the climb, from the work in the dark, have scabbed over. They are stiff. They ache with a dull, familiar throb. The ache is a memory. It is the memory of work. It is not the work itself.

I turn from the grave. My eyes are drawn upward, along the line of the cliff. Above the cemetery, the rock face is sheer for a hundred feet, then breaks into a jagged outcrop. There is a cleft there. A shadow. From this angle, in this light, it looks like the socket of a giant’s eye.

I know that cleft. I have seen it from the inside. It is the window of the bell tower. The place where I stood not an hour ago, my hand on the silent bell.

From here, the tower is not a part of the abbey. It is a part of the mountain. A fang of worked stone grown from the living rock. The bell inside is not a thing hung by men. It is a fruit. A metal seed waiting.

The hook is gone.

But my eyes are caught on that high, dark socket. My feet, without my telling them, begin to move. Not back toward the abbey gate. Toward the cliff base, below the tower. The goat track peters out into a scramble of boulders and ice. I climb. It is not the desperate, hooked climbing of before. It is mechanical. My body seeking occupation. My mind is quiet. It is watching.

I reach a ledge. It is broad enough to stand on. The tower wall rises directly above me, sheer and smooth. The foundation stones here are massive, bigger than a man. They are the color of old bone. I put my hand against one. It is cold. The true cold. The cold that drinks.

And I feel it.

Not a hook. Not a pull.

A resonance.

It is so faint it is almost not there. A vibration in the stone, or in the marrow of my own bones. It is not the cup’s hum. That was a wrong note, a string tuned too tight, screaming. This is the opposite. It is a note missing. A silence within the silence. A gap in the scale.

I close my eyes. I press my forehead to the stone. The cold bites.

I listen.

I am not listening with my ears. I am listening with the place where the hook was rooted. The hollow.

And in that hollow, something echoes.

It is not a sound. It is the memory of a sound. The ghost of a vibration that has not yet happened. It is the shape a note would make if it were born from this stone, from this mountain, from this new, clean silence.

My breath catches. It fogs on the rock.

I understand.

The cup’s question was: *Remember me.*
My question is: *What do I make now?*

The mountain is not asking for memory. It is not asking for a story to be finished. That story is written. The last page is turned.

The mountain is asking for a new story. A first note.

The bell in the tower above me is my brother’s bell. It is a good bell. It is true. But it is his. It was born from his hollow, his need. It answered the question of his life.

It is not mine.

The resonance I feel in the stone is not a call for silence. It is a call for answer.

A bell does not answer a question with words. It answers with a note. A single, true vibration that shapes the air, that defines the silence around it. It says: *I am here.*

I step back from the wall. My hands are no longer empty. They are full of the idea of a shape. The hollow in my chest is no longer empty. It is a mold.

I will make a bell.

Not for an abbey. Not for a order. Not to call the faithful to prayer.

I will make a bell for the mountain. For the silence after the fever. A bell that is the first note of what comes after. A bell that is not a memory, but a beginning.

The thought is clean. It is sharp. It cuts through the numbness like a chisel through soft wax.

I turn and half-slide, half-climb down the boulders to the path. My blood is moving again. It is not the frantic river of fear. It is the purposeful flow of a man who has seen the next mountain. The cold air is no longer just cold. It is material. It is the thing my bell will one day move.

I enter the abbey grounds through the postern gate. The novices are still sweeping, still carrying. I pass them. I go directly to the workshop. My workshop. It has been days since I stood inside it.

The room is as I left it. Cold. The forge is dark. The tools lie where I dropped them in my haste to follow the hook. The half-finished bell-clapper for the village church lies on the bench, a dumb lump of bronze. It is dead work. It belongs to the before.

I walk to the back wall. To the shelves where the raw materials are kept. Ingots of tin. Bars of copper. My brother’s secret ledger, where he recorded his alloys, his temperatures, his prayers. I take down a tin ingot. It is heavy. Dense. Its surface is smooth and cool. I take down a copper bar. The color of a new penny, of heart’s blood.

I carry them to the great central table. I lay them side by side. Tin and copper. The parents of bronze.

This will be my alloy. But it will not be my brother’s recipe. His was for clarity, for reach, for a note that would pierce cloud and carry prayer to heaven. Mine must be for something else. For depth. For grounding. For a note that does not seek the sky, but speaks to the stone. A note that says *here* to the *here*.

I need clay. For the core. For the cope. I need hair from the tails of horses, to bind the clay. I need wax. For the false bell, the model that will be melted away to leave the shape of the emptiness.

I have none of these things.

The realization is a bucket of well-water. It does not douse the fire, but it defines its bounds. I have a vision. I have metal. I have a need. I do not have the means.

I stand in the cold workshop, my hands on the cold metal, and I laugh. The sound is short, harsh. It echoes off the stone walls. It is the first true sound I have made since I climbed out of the undercroft.

I am a bell-maker. This is the making. The first step is not the ringing. It is the gathering. The asking.

I go to find the Prior.

He is in the village. It is near dusk when he returns. He comes into the workshop as I am cleaning the bench, making a space for the new work. He looks older. The lines on his face are not just from worry now, but from a weary relief. He carries the weight of a burden that has been set down. It has left its grooves in him.

“The village is whole,” he says, leaning in the doorway. “The cracks in the walls can be mended. No one was lost. They are… quiet. They feel the silence. They do not know what to do with it.”

“They will learn,” I say.

He looks at the ingots on the table. His eyes are shrewd. “You are not leaving.”

“No.”

“You are starting new work.”

“Yes.”

He steps into the room. He runs a finger along the edge of the copper bar. “A bell.”

“Yes.”

“For whom?”

“For the mountain.”

He is silent for a long time. He looks from the metal to my face, then to the dark mouth of the forge. “The abbey coffers are not deep. We have the tithe from the village, but it is barley and wool and salt. Not coin.”

“I do not need coin,” I say. “I need clay. I need wax. I need horsehair. I need charcoal for the forge. I need help. A strong back to turn the crucible. A careful hand to help with the molding.”

“And in return?”

“The bell,” I say. “When it is cast, when it is tuned, it will hang here. In your tower, or in a new tower, if you build one. It will be the abbey’s bell. But its voice… its voice will be for the mountain. It will be the first note of what comes after.”

“A memorial,” he says.

“No,” I say, and the word comes out harder than I mean. “A memorial looks back. This must look forward. It is not for the dead singers. It is for the living silence.”

He understands. I see it in the slight loosening of his shoulders. He is a man of faith. He understands that a void demands a shape. That a silence, to be holy, must be defined by sound.

“You shall have what you need,” he says. “The village will give the hair from their plough-horses. The wax we have from our own hives. The clay is in the riverbank. The charcoal… we will cut wood and make it. The brothers will help. It will be work for their hands. A good work.”

He turns to go, then pauses. “And the alloy? Your brother’s mix?”

“My own,” I say.

He nods, once. “Then it will be your bell.”

When he is gone, the night settles into the workshop. I light a single candle. Its flame is a tiny, trembling bell of light in the vast dark. I sit at the table with my brother’s ledger. I open it to a blank page at the back.

I take up a charcoal stick.

I do not write a recipe. I draw. I draw the curve of the sound-bow. The slope of the waist. The crown. I draw it not as it will look, but as it must *be*. The lines are not just lines. They are the paths the vibration will take. The shape is not just a shape. It is a map of a note.

This is the first act of making. The imagining. The creation of the ghost that will guide the hands.

I work until the candle gutters. The shape on the page is crude. It is a child’s sketch of a bell. But to me, it is alive. It is the answer forming in the hollow.

I blow out the candle. In the sudden dark, the ghost of the flame dances on my vision. I close my eyes. I see the bell. Not the drawing. The true bell. It hangs in a tower of light and air. It is still. It is waiting.

And in the darkness behind my eyes, I feel the faint, sure resonance from the mountain’s stone. It is not a pull. It is a question, tuned to a frequency only my hollow can hear.

It asks: *What shape will your answer take?*

I open my eyes. The workshop is black. The metal on the table is invisible.

But I know it is there.

And I know its shape.

The silence is patient. It is waiting for the first note.

But first, it must wait for the gathering. For the clay, the wax, the hair. For the building of the furnace. For the long, slow, dirty work of making the mold. For the prayer of the fire. For the birth of the blank.

The making has begun.

And I realize, with a certainty that is colder and harder than the copper on the table, that I do not know how to make this bell. My brother’s methods were for church bells. For bells that call across distances. This bell must call inward. It must resonate with stone, not with sky. The alloy must be different. The shape must be different. The thickness of the sound-bow, the taper of the wall… all of it is a mystery.

I have never made a bell like this. No one has.

The hollow in my chest yawns wider. It is not just empty. It is daunted.

I stand in the dark. The ghost of the bell on the page is already wrong. I know it. I felt it as I drew. The lines did not sing. They were silent marks.

This is the second step of the making. The step after the vision. The step where you realize your hands are ignorant. Your knowledge is a ladder that does not reach the place you need to go.

I need a new ladder.

I need to understand the mountain’s bone. I need to understand the silence I am meant to break.

I leave the workshop. The abbey is asleep. The moon is a sliver of white ice, sharp enough to cut the night. I walk again, through the cloister, out the gate, to the cliff below the tower. I do not climb. I stand on the path. I look up at the black socket of the tower window.

I listen.

The resonance is there. Fainter now, almost swallowed by the vast, star-dusted quiet of the night. It is not a note. It is a question made of stone and cold and time.

My brother learned from our father. I learned from my brother. I have the language of bells in my hands. But this bell requires a different language. A tongue of geology and echo. Of pressure and release.

I cannot learn it here. Not from these stones, which have been cut and shaped by men. I need the raw stone. The unmade mountain.

I turn my back on the abbey. I look east, to where the mountain ridge climbs higher, into the wild places where no paths go. Where the rock is not shaped for altars, but for wind and avalanche.

The hook is gone.

But a new compass is forming. It does not pull. It points.

It points away from the made world. Into the unmade.

The bell will not be born from a recipe in a ledger. It will be born from a listening. A deep, patient listening to the thing it must answer.

I will go into the high places. I will take my hammer and my ear. I will strike the living rock and I will listen to its voice. I will learn the note that is already there, sleeping in the stone. And I will shape my bell to wake it.

This is not gathering materials. This is gathering knowledge. The most fragile material of all.

I look at my hands in the moonlight. They are empty. They are ready to learn a new shape.

The first note is not cast in bronze.

It is heard in the bone of the world.

And I must go and listen for it.

The patient silence of the abbey is behind me. Ahead is a different silence. The hungry, waiting silence of the heights. It is not a silence after a story. It is the silence before the first word of a story that has never been told.

I take a step toward it.

And the hollow in my chest rings, once, faintly, like a struck crystal.

It is the sound of a decision hardening.

It is the hook, remade.



---

# Chapter 18

## CHAPTER 18

The mountain does not care for footsteps. That is the first true thing. The scree under my boot is a cascade of indifference. It slides, it chatters, it settles into a new arrangement of itself. It does not remember my passing. I climb not on a path, but on the memory of paths, on animal traces that dissolve into rockfall and thorn. The silence of the heights is not empty. It is a mouth full of teeth. Wind through a high pass. The scrabble of a pika. The creak of my own knees, a sound so small the mountain swallows it whole.

I have been climbing for three days. The patient silence of the abbey is a remembered dream, a stifled room. This silence is a presence. It leans against my ears. It watches from the grey faces of cliffs. I am a mote in its eye.

My pack is light. A hammer. A chisel. A water-skin. A wedge of hard cheese wrapped in cloth. The ledger, its pages blank now, waiting for a new language. I left the bell-metal in the undercroft. It is a made thing. It belongs to the made world. I need the unmade.

On the second day, I found a vein of stone that sang. Not with music. With a dry, percussive crack. I struck it with the hammer. The sound was a dead click. A closed door. I moved on.

I am looking for a different song. The one my hollow chest heard. The resonance that is not a note, but a question. I climb to listen. Each step is a listening. The wind is a constant exhale, but beneath it, in the still spaces between gusts, the mountain speaks in the language of settling. A pebble drops. A root sighs. A seam of ice, hidden from the sun, groans as it thaws.

I make camp on a ledge under an overhang. The stone here is dark, streaked with lichen like old bruises. I build no fire. The cold is a clean cold. It bites, it clarifies. It is the cold my body remembers from the undercroft, but here it is honest. It does not hide a rotting sweetness. It smells of pine and void.

I eat the cheese. I drink a little water. The night folds over the mountain, a black cloth stitched with stars so sharp they seem to cut the sky. I lie back on the stone. It is cold. Properly cold. It drinks my warmth. I close my eyes.

And I remember my brother’s hands.

Not as they were at the end—stiff, waxen—but as they were in the workshop. Alive with knowledge. He could tap a rough ingot of bronze and know its heart. He could hear the impurities, the pockets of tin, the whispers of other metals. His ear was a tuning fork for the unseen.

“You listen with your skin, too,” he said once. His voice is clear in this high, thin air. A memory not dragged up, but given. “The sound travels up the hammer. Into your wrist. Into the bone of your arm. You feel the bell’s truth before you hear it.”

I had nodded. I had thought I understood.

I did not understand.

I was listening for a song. He was listening for a soul.

The hollow in my chest is quiet. It is a cup waiting to be filled. I press my palm flat against the rock of my ledge. I feel nothing but cold hardness. A dead thing.

But my brother’s voice persists. *You feel the truth before you hear it.*

I sit up. The stars wheel. I take my hammer. I do not strike the rock. I lay the iron head gently against the stone, then press my ear to the hammer’s haft.

I listen.

At first, there is only the thud of my own blood. The distant wind. Then, a low, almost imperceptible hum. A vibration so deep it is less a sound than a tremor in the tooth. It is the mountain breathing. The slow, tectonic sigh of weight and time. It is not a note. It is the bedrock of all notes.

I understand. This is not the song. This is the silence the song must break.

I pull the hammer away. The memory of the vibration lingers in my jaw. A false victory sparks, dry as tinder. I have found the listening place. I have heard the world’s bone. I can begin.

The falsehood of it is so complete I almost laugh. The sound is bitter in my throat.

I have heard nothing. I have imposed a memory of understanding onto a sensation. I have built my brother’s ghost into the hum of stone. This is not knowledge. This is nostalgia. A reflection in a dark pool.

The collapse is quiet. It is the collapse of a premise. I came here to listen to the mountain, to learn the note sleeping in the stone. But I brought my own need with me. My own empty shape. I am trying to fill my hollow with an echo of my brother’s skill. I am not listening to the mountain. I am listening for him.

The identity I have carried—the bell-founder, the keeper of the ledger, the brother’s shadow—is useless here. It is a garment for the lowlands. Up here, the wind strips it away. What is left is not a founder. It is a man with a hollow chest, chasing a sound he cannot name, using tools that are suddenly childish. A hammer. A chisel. Forged things. They belong to the world of hooks and towers and recipes.

The night wears on. The cold deepens. I do not sleep. I watch the stars bleach the sky. My decision, which had hardened like crystal in my chest at the abbey gate, feels brittle now. A thing that could shatter with a single misstep.

When the grey light comes, it does not bring warmth. It reveals the scale of my arrogance. The mountain range stretches east, peak after peak, a frozen sea of rock and snow. I am a beetle on the spine of a giant. My plan is a beetle’s plan.

But the hook is remade. It is not pulling me forward. It is a barb in my spirit, turning me inward. The journey is not into the mountain. It is into the nature of the hollow itself.

I shoulder my pack. The hammer is a foolish weight. I climb.

The way grows steeper. The air thinner. My breath saws in my throat. I find no more veins of song. I find only endurance. The scrape of boot on rock. The burn in my thighs. The raw identity of muscle and lung. I am not a listener. I am a climber. A body moving against gravity. It is a pure, stupid truth. I cling to it.

Near midday, I come to a place where the mountain has been torn open. A cliff face sheared away, perhaps by ice, perhaps by some ancient grief in the earth. The raw wound of it is a sheer wall, fifty feet high, glistening with damp where the sun never touches. At its base, a tumble of fresh-looking rockfall, sharp-edged and unwearied by weather.

This is unmade stone. Not a vein, but a viscera.

I approach. The air is still here, trapped in the lee of the cliff. It smells of crushed flint and something else. A metallic tang, like the smell of lightning after it strikes ground.

I kneel by a fallen slab. It is a different grey than the surrounding rock. Darker. Denser. I run my fingers over it. It is not porous. It is seamless. I pick up a smaller fist-sized piece. It is heavy for its size. Cold seeps from it into my palm.

This is not singing stone.

This is listening stone.

I understand the difference in my bones, before the thought forms. This stone does not want to give a note. It wants to receive one. It is the ear of the mountain, turned inward.

I take my hammer. I do not press my ear to the haft. I do not try to listen with my brother’s memory. I simply strike the small stone in my hand against the larger slab at my feet.

The sound is not a crack. Not a click.

It is a *thock*.

A soft, profound, absorbent sound. A drop of water falling into a deep well. The stone swallows the impact. It does not ring. It does not resonate. It accepts. And in that acceptance, there is a quality… a finality. A silence that is not an absence, but a conclusion.

The hollow in my chest does not ring.

It *still*.

For a single, vertiginous heartbeat, the emptiness is not a lack. It is a shape. A vessel. Not for a bell-note, but for this… this absorbent silence. This listening quality.

This is the knowledge I came to gather. Not how to make a bell sing, but what a bell must answer. It must answer a silence like this. A silence that eats sound. A silence that is not peace, but a kind of hungry void.

My false victory is ashes. My quest is upended. I did not need to find the mountain’s song. I needed to find its silence. The specific, hungry silence that lives in this unmade place.

I look at the dark, dense stone. This is the material. Not for the bell. For the mold. The void into which the molten metal must be poured. The bell must be shaped by this silence. It must be born from this absorbent dark.

I have been thinking like a founder. I must think like a miner. I must take this listening stone back down the mountain.

I wedge my chisel into a seam on the slab. I raise my hammer.

And a voice says, “That will not come free.”

The voice comes from above me. It is dry as the rockfall. It is not loud, but the still air carries it perfectly.

I freeze. The hammer’s weight is suddenly immense. I look up.

A man sits on a ledge partway up the shear cliff face. I did not see him. He is the colour of the stone. His clothes are patched and faded. His hair is a wild grey mane. He is perched like a bird of prey, knees drawn up, arms resting on them. He has been watching me.

“It is rooted,” he says. His eyes are chips of the same dark stone. “The piece you hold is a tooth. The jaw is deep.”

I lower the hammer. My heart is a frantic thing, caged in my ribs. “Who are you?”

He considers the question as if it is in a foreign tongue. “I am here,” he says, finally. “You are here. The stone is here. Names are for the valleys.”

“Do you live here?”

“Live.” He tastes the word. “I am not dead. The stone is not dead. We are… present.” He tilts his head. “You are loud. Your thoughts are loud. They bang against the quiet.”

I have no answer. The climb, the revelation, this apparition—they have stripped me of language.

He shifts, a slight movement like a settling of rock. “You seek the heart-sound. The note to break the great quiet.”

It is not a question. A statement of weathered fact.

“How do you know that?”

“You listen with your whole body. A man who listens like that is either a madman or a man with a hole in him.” His stone-chip eyes seem to look directly at the hollow in my chest. “You do not have the eyes of a madman. You have the eyes of a man who has lost his reflection.”

The accuracy of it is a blow. I sink back onto my heels. “I need this stone.”

“For what?”

“To make a mold. To cast a bell that can answer… this.” I gesture at the cliff, the silence.

“Answer,” he echoes, and there is a thread of something in his voice. Not amusement. Something older. “You think the silence asks a question?”

“Doesn’t it?”

“The silence *is* the answer,” he says. “To every question. The rock knows this. The ice knows this. You valley-people make your noises—your bells, your shouts, your prayers—to pretend the answer is not already here. To pretend you have not heard it.”

He stands. He is taller than I expected, and he moves with a fluid grace that belies his raggedness. In two steps and a leap, he is down the rockfall, landing soundlessly a few paces from me.

He points a long, grimy finger at the dark stone in my hand. “That is the ear. You wish to make a mouth. A mouth to shout at an ear that is already full of the only truth. The truth of an ending.” He comes closer. His smell is of dust and dried herbs. “Why?”

The word hangs in the cold air. It is the only question that matters.

And I do not know. To finish my brother’s work? To fill my hollow? To prove I learned the language? They are all reflections in a dead pool. They are valley-reasons.

I look at the listening stone. I think of the *thock* sound. The finality.

“I think,” I say, the words forming slowly, like ice on still water, “the shout must be so pure, it becomes the same as the silence. Not an answer. An… echo of the answer. So the ear can recognize itself.”

The man stares at me. For a long time, he is still as the cliff. Then, a slow crack appears in his weathered face. A smile. It is a rare, difficult thing.

“Ah,” he says. The syllable is a release of breath. “You are not just loud. You are a ripple in a deep pool. You wish to make the stone hear its own listening.”

He turns and places his hand flat on the dark slab, where I had set my chisel. “This will not come free with that.” He nods at my hammer. “It must be persuaded. It must be shown the shape of its own absence.”

He looks at me. “You have the shape of an absence in you. Show it.”

I do not understand. I hold out the fist-sized piece of dark stone. “This?”

“No. The shape in your chest. The hollow bell of you. Press it there.” He points to the slab.

It is madness. But I have climbed beyond the borders of sense. I step forward. I place my left hand, the one not holding the stone, against the cold, dense rock. I close my eyes. I do not think of my brother. I do not think of bells. I think of the hollow. I imagine it not as an emptiness, but as a precise, inverted shape. A bell-shaped void. I press that imagined shape, that feeling of resonant absence, into the palm of my hand. I push it into the stone.

I am a fool. I am performing for a hermit on a mountain.

The man places his hand over mine. His skin is rough as sandstone. “Now,” he whispers. “Ask for it.”

I have no words. Only the shape of the hollow. I push it, silently, into the unyielding dark.

And something gives.

Not in the stone. In me.

A memory, not of my brother, but of my father. A memory I did not know I had. He is not at the forge. He is in a field, at dusk. He is holding me, a small child, on his shoulders. I am afraid of the descending dark. He points to the first star, a prick of light in the violet sky.

“See that?” he says, his voice a rumble I feel through his body into mine. “That is a nail. The sky is a bell, and the stars are the nails that hold its shape against the dark. Without them, the dark would swallow it whole. Remember that. The nail is as important as the bell.”

The memory is a shard of glass in my mind. I have never remembered this. It is not a memory of craft. It is a memory of metaphor. Of a way to see the world. My father gave me that, long before my brother gave me the language of hammers.

The hollow in my chest *rings*.

A single, clear, silent vibration.

And under my palm, the stone *sighs*.

A fine crack weaves through the slab, not a break, but a separation. A perfect, bell-shaped section, the size of a large cauldron, detaches from the whole. It leans forward, an inch, two inches, held by nothing but its own new shape.

The hermit removes his hand. He steps back. His stone-chip eyes are wide. “You did not lie,” he says, softly. “You carry a true hollow.”

I am trembling. The released slab stands, a dark, dense promise. My father’s voice echoes in the new chamber of the memory. *The nail is as important as the bell.*

I understand now. The bell I must cast is not the answer. It is the nail. It must hold the shape of the question against the consuming silence. It must give the silence a form to press against. To define itself against.

This dark stone will be the mold. The bell will be born from the mountain’s own listening. It will be a nail of bronze, driven into the ear of the void.

I have my material. Not by force. By recognition.

I turn to the hermit. “Thank you.”

He shakes his head. “Do not thank me. Thank the absence you carry. It is a key.” He looks east, to the higher, wilder peaks. “But a key turns both ways. You have opened the stone. The stone has opened you. You have remembered something. Something the valley made you forget.”

He is right. The memory of my father is fresh and aching. It is an identity prior to brother, prior to founder. A child on shoulders, learning about stars and nails.

“What do I owe you?” I ask.

“Owe?” He seems puzzled. “You owe the stone your respect. You owe the silence your bell. You owe yourself the truth of your hollow.” He gestures to the detached slab. “Take it. It is yours. It has chosen to go with you.”

The work is backbreaking. With ropes and leverage and the hermit’s silent, expert help, we lower the bell-shaped section of stone from the cliff. It is impossibly heavy. It will take me weeks to get it down the mountain. It will require sledges, rollers, a different kind of endurance.

As we work, the hermit speaks little. But as the sun begins to bleed into the western ridges, he says, “The man you learned from. Your brother?”

“Yes.”

“He knew of nails and bells?”

“He knew of bells.”

“But not of nails.” The hermit nods, as if confirming something. “A bell without a nail is a song that forgets its own shape. It becomes just another noise. Then, just another silence.” He looks at me. “You are not your brother. You are the child on the shoulders. Remember which one saw the sky first.”

The finality in his voice is like the *thock* of the stone. It settles in me.

When the slab is secured on a crude sledge of pine branches, the hermit melts back into the cliff face. He does not say goodbye. He simply is, and then he is not.

I am alone again with the listening stone and the hungry silence.

I have won a victory. I have the heart of the mold. I have a new understanding. The collapse of my old purpose has birthed a clearer, harder one.

But as I prepare to begin the long, brutal descent, the hook in my spirit twists.

The memory of my father is a key, yes. It has opened a door in the stone and in my past.

But a key also locks.

What door have I just locked behind me?

The identity of the brother’s shadow, the faithful apprentice, the keeper of the ledger—that door is shut. I am something else now. A man with a father’s metaphor in his heart and a mountain’s silence in his mold.

I look at my hands in the dying light. They are cut, bruised, dirty. They are no longer a founder’s hands. They are a miner’s hands. A mover of stone. They have learned a new shape.

The first note is not cast in bronze.

It is heard in the bone of the world.

And I have heard it. It is the sound of a memory, long buried, cracking open like stone.

I take the first step down, the ropes of the sledge biting into my shoulders. The weight is immense. It is the weight of the unmade world, agreeing to be shaped.

The hollow in my chest is quiet. It is no longer a question. It is a mold, waiting to be filled.

And as I descend, leaving the highest silence behind, a colder thought follows me, a shadow to the new light.

My brother never spoke of our father’s metaphors. He spoke only of recipes, of temperatures, of proportions.

Did he forget?

Or did he choose not to remember?

The child on the shoulders saw the sky first.

But who was it that later became afraid of the dark?



---

# Chapter 19

## CHAPTER 19

The descent is a forgetting. The mountain gives back its stolen cold, breath by breath, as I lower myself through its ribs. The stone on its sledge is a dead star, its gravity warping the path. Each step is a negotiation with the slope. The rope is a burning thread sawing into the meat of my shoulder, a counterpoint to the old ache in my side. The pain is a ledger. It keeps the accounts of this journey. Bruise for understanding. Cut for revelation. The deep, muscular fire for the weight of the metaphor I now carry.

I do not think of the hermit’s cliff, or the sky. I think of the next handhold, the next placement of my foot on scree that shifts like loose grain. The world narrows to the tension in the rope, the groan of the pine branches beneath the slab, the scent of crushed moss and my own salt sweat. This is the work. The mind empties into the body. The questions, those sharp and glittering things, are packed away like delicate tools. They would unbalance me here.

But the mountain remembers for me. It speaks in echoes.

The first echo is the wind. It changes as I descend. On the high silence, it was a blade, honed on ice. Here, in the lee of the great rock shoulder, it becomes a voice. It funnels through a crack in the cliff face, and the sound is not a howl. It is a hum. A single, sustained note, pitched just below hearing. I feel it in my molars. It is the bone of the world, singing.

My brother would have called it a draught. A flaw in the stone. He would have noted it, quantified the likely wind speed and direction, estimated its effect on the stability of the slope. A practical fact. A problem to be solved or a factor to be weighed.

I stop. The rope goes slack. I lean my forehead against the cold rock beside the fissure. The hum enters through the skin of my brow. It vibrates the memory loose.

*My father’s workshop, deep in a summer afternoon. The furnace is sleeping, a banked red eye. He is at his bench, not working. Listening. His head is cocked. “Hear that?”*

*I hear nothing but flies against the windowpane, the distant shout of a cart vendor in the street.*

*“The city,” he says, his voice a whisper of awe. “It has a note. Today… it is an F. A flat, grumbling F. The carts on the cobbles are the bow, the stones are the string.” He closes his eyes. “Your mother’s walk from the well… that’s a grace note. A flicker of A.”*

*I try to hear it. I hear only noise. Chaos. I look at my brother, across the bench, polishing a crucible with meticulous, silent circles. He does not look up. He hears nothing either. Or he chooses not to.*

*“Everything that is, sings,” my father says, opening his eyes. They are bright with the secret. “To cast a bell is not to create a note. It is to trap one of the world’s many songs. To give it a permanent home.”*

I pull my head from the rock. The hum is still there. The mountain’s F, or its B, or its note beyond naming. My brother never spoke of this. He spoke of recipes. Of temperatures. Of proportions.

Did he forget?

Or did he choose not to remember?

The question is a pebble dropped into the well of my descent. I hear it strike the sides, all the way down.

***

I make camp in a cup of stone as the light bleeds from iron-grey to charcoal. The slab is anchored, the sledge lashed to a stubborn finger of rock. I eat the last of the hermit’s pressed cake. It tastes of bark and a faint, floral honey. The hollow in my chest is not hungry. It is patient. A mold, waiting to be filled.

Sleep, when it comes, is not a blankness. It is a cellar.

I am in the undercroft again. But it is wrong. The stone is warm. The air is dry and sweet with rot. I am lying on my back, feeling the grit of the lukewarm tooth against my shoulder blade. Footsteps above. Not the Prior’s. Not a stranger’s. These are familiar. They are the measured, precise steps of my brother, pacing the flagstones of the chapel. I can see them, in the way you see things in dreams: through the solid stone, as if it is murky water.

He is not pacing in prayer. He is measuring. I hear the soft, regular tap of his staff—the one he used after the leg injury—striking the joins between the stones. *Tap. Step. Tap. Step.* He is counting. He is mapping the silence.

And he is speaking. His voice comes down through the stone, filtered and distorted, but it is his.

“The foundation note is G,” he says, to no one, to the empty chapel. “A solid, stupid G. The root. The plodding footstep.” *Tap.* “The heresy of the south transept arch… that aspires to an E-flat. A wistful, doomed reach.” *Tap.* “But the undercroft…”

The tapping stops. Right above me.

“The undercroft is a rest. A fermata. It holds the breath of the stone. It is where the music goes to forget itself.”

In the dream, I try to call up to him. *I am here. I hear it too.* My mouth is sealed with dust.

His voice drops, becomes a confidential murmur to the shadows. “He thought music was something you added. Like gilt. A decoration for the silence. He was wrong. The silence *is* the music. The rest is the point. The foundation is the pause upon which the fragile melody of faith is built. You do not build a bell to break a silence. You build it to *honor* the silence. To frame it. To make it audible.”

I feel a hand on the iron ring of the undercroft door. Not to enter. To feel the vibration.

“You forgot that, little brother,” the dream-voice whispers, now clear as if his lips are at my ear. “You chased the note. I stayed with the rest.”

I wake with a gasp. The mountain air is knife-cold. The dream clings like a cobweb. The words are not memory. They are invention. My brother never said such things. He spoke of recipes. Of temperatures. Of proportions.

But the certainty in the dream-voice… it has the texture of truth. It fits the lock of my new suspicion.

He heard the music. He chose the ledger.

Why?

***

The second day of descent is a passage through the body of the mountain. The path, such as it is, tunnels through a chaos of fallen boulders, a cataclysm frozen in time. The stone giants lean against one another, their faces scarred by ancient weather. Here, the world is all angles and crushing weight. The sky is a ragged blue thread, far above.

I am a beetle in the rubble. The slab on its sledge is a stubborn, scraping companion. We move through a silence that is not true silence. It is a silence of pressure, of held breath. The wind’s hum is gone. In its place is the trickle of meltwater, a sound like a ticking clock. My own breathing, loud in the stone vault.

This is the architecture of collapse. My brother would have seen instability. Calculated load. Assessed risk.

I see a cathedral of accident.

And then, I see the mark.

It is on the flank of a vast, tilted block of granite, sheltered from the worst of the weather. A symbol. Chiseled, not by nature. The lines are too straight, the curves too intentional. It is worn, smoothed by centuries of wind and lichen, but unmistakable.

It is the Founders’ Mark. But not my father’s. Not my brother’s. It is older. The primary sigil is the same—the cup, the hammer, the flame—but the personal glyph beneath it is one I have seen only in the brittle pages of the oldest guild ledger in the workshop. *Gervais the Unflinching.* He worked four centuries ago. His bells are legends. They are the ones that cracked in the Great Frost, or were melted down for cannon, or whose songs are said to have quelled riots.

He was here.

He came this way. He left his name on the bone of the world.

I run my fingers over the grooves. The stone is cold here, properly cold. It drinks the warmth from my fingertips. This is the cold I remember. The cold of the undercroft that should have been. This mark is an anchor. A proof. I am not the first to walk this path with a founder’s heart. The knowledge is a warmth in my chest, separate from the muscle-fire of labor.

Gervais the Unflinching. Did he come for a stone? For a silence? Did he hear a note in the high air that needed a home?

The mark is a whisper in the collapse. *You are not lost. You are following.*

It changes the descent. It is no longer a forgetting. It is a reading. I begin to look for other signs. Not marks, but intentions. The way a particular cleft frames a distant peak—a deliberate window. The way the water-tick-tick echoes in a certain chamber of rock—a natural metronome. Gervais may have passed here once. But someone else has been here more recently. The path, under the scree, has a logic. A faint suggestion of choice.

I think of the hermit. *The first ones to come here were not hiding. They were listening.*

This is a listening place.

And my brother, who spoke of recipes and proportions, sent me here to die.

The two facts do not fit. They grind against each other in my mind, creating a friction that is hotter than the rope-burn on my shoulder.

He sent me to a sacred place. A founder’s pilgrimage. Was it a final, twisted gift? A poisoned sacrament? Or did he, the keeper of the ledger, the man of the rest, believe the silence here would simply absorb me? That I, the child who chased the note, would be dissolved into the great fermata?

The hook in my spirit twists. It is no longer a simple hook of betrayal. It is more complex. A barbed question.

Did he hate me? Or did he, in some corrupted way, revere me enough to offer me this as a terminus?

***

The stone forest gives way to a slope of ancient, compacted scree. The going is faster, more treacherous. The sledge skids and bucks behind me. My world is the rope, the skittering stones, the burning in my thighs.

I do not hear them until they are close.

Sound comes strangely here. It is the scrape of boot on rock, thirty paces to my left and above. A sound that is not the wind, not the water, not the settling of the mountain.

I freeze. The rope goes taut. I become a stone among stones.

There are two of them. They are moving with a purpose that is neither pilgrimage nor desperate flight. It is the purpose of the hunt. A controlled, scanning pace. They are not speaking. Their silence is professional.

I see the glint of metal. A sword hilt. The dull sheen of a boiled leather brigandine. Not soldiers. Not in uniform. But not bandits. Their movement is too coordinated, too economical.

They are between me and the tree line, the beginning of the lower forest that leads back to the world of roads and inns and questions.

I crouch behind a boulder, my hand on the rough granite. My heart is the frantic counter-rhythm to the mountain’s slow beat. They have not seen me. The slab and its sledge are hidden in the lee of a larger rock.

They pause. One of them, a man with a grey-flecked beard, kneels. He points at the ground. My ground. The path I have just made through the loose scree.

He has found my tracks.

The other, younger, with a face like a hatchet, follows the line of the tracks upward, directly toward my hiding place. His hand goes to his sword.

This is not chance. They are not shepherds or hunters strayed high. They are looking. For a man. A man descending a mountain with a great weight.

My brother’s finality. *Thock.* The sound of the stone settling.

Did it also signal the releasing of the hounds?

The logic is cold and clear. He gave me a purpose. A sacred, impossible task. And he ensured I would not return from it. A pilgrim should complete his journey, or vanish into the sanctity he seeks. He would not have hired common cutthroats. He would have hired men who understood silence. Men who could move through a landscape like a bad thought.

The younger one takes a step toward my boulder. I look at my hands, cut, bruised, dirty. Miner’s hands. A mover of stone.

I am not a fighter. I am a founder. But the founder in me is gone. The man on the mountain is something else. Something that has heard the bone-note and traced the mark of Gervais and carried a dead star down a slope of forgetting.

I let go of the rope.

I pick up a stone. Not just any stone. A piece of the mountain the size of my fist, jagged, dense. It is not a weapon. It is a sample. A fragment of the greater silence.

I do not throw it.

I stand up.

The movement is calm. Deliberate. I step out from behind the boulder, into their line of sight.

They start. The younger one’s sword comes out halfway with a soft *shing*. The older one’s hand goes up, a signal to wait. They stare. I must be a wild thing—filthy, hair matted, clothes torn, eyes set in a face grimy with sweat and dust. I am holding a rock.

“You are off the path,” I say. My voice is rough from disuse, but it does not shake. It is the voice of the mountain. Dry. Gritty.

The older one recovers first. “We are looking for a man. A founder. He may be in distress. Have you seen him?”

His words are courteous. His eyes are not. They are scanning me, my empty hands, the rock, the space behind me for the thing I must be dragging.

“I have seen no one,” I say. “This is a listening place. Men make too much noise.”

The younger one sneers. “He’s mad. A hermit.”

The older one ignores him. He takes a step closer. “You have the look of a man who has been climbing. Or descending. Your hands.” He nods at them. “Those are not a hermit’s hands. They are a worker’s hands.”

“I listen with my hands,” I say. It is not a lie. “The stone speaks through the skin.”

I see his calculation. A madman is unpredictable. A madman with a rock is a trivial threat. But a madman might have seen his quarry. He needs information.

“The man we seek,” the older one says, taking another cautious step. “He would be carrying something. A burden. A piece of stone.”

I look past him, to the distant tree line. I feel the weight of the slab behind me, its presence screaming in the silence. He is close enough now. I can see the pores of his nose, the scar on his chin.

“A burden,” I repeat, as if the word is foreign. I look at the rock in my hand. I hold it up between us. “This is a burden. It carries the memory of fire. Can you hear it?”

I am my father’s son. I am speaking a metaphor. It is my only weapon.

He frowns. Impatience flickers. The performance is not working. He is not a man for metaphors. He is a man for tracks and swords and completed tasks.

“Enough,” the younger one snaps. He pushes past the older, his sword now fully drawn. “Where is the stone? What have you done with it?”

He charges the last few paces.

I do not throw the rock at him.

I drop it.

I drop it onto a larger, flat slab of granite at my feet.

*CRACK.*

The sound is shocking in the compressed air. It is not a loud sound. It is a *final* sound. A cleaving. A decision.

The reaction is not in the men.

It is in the mountain.

The flat slab I struck is a keystone. A loose, precarious lid over a chimney of scree. I saw it when I chose my hiding place. I read the intention of the collapse.

The *crack* is the trigger.

The slab shifts. It tilts. With a sigh that is the sound of a thousand tiny avalances, the slope below it—the slope between the two men and me—begins to move.

It is not a cataclysm. It is a subsidence. A sudden, rushing river of fist-sized stones and gravel and dust. It flows between us, a brown, chattering torrent, cutting the space in two.

The younger man stumbles back with a curse. The older one is lost in a cloud of dust.

I do not wait. I am already moving. Not back to my slab. Away from it. Up the slope, diagonally, across the grain of the slide. I move like the goat I have become, leaping from stable rock to stable rock, my path invisible to them in the chaos.

I leave the slab. I leave the sledge. I leave the rope.

I take only the knowledge of where they are.

I hear their shouts, muffled by the river of stone. They are not cries of pain. They are cries of frustration. They are on the wrong side of the moving scree. By the time it settles, I will be a ghost in the stone forest.

I climb until my lungs scream. I find a perch behind a tooth of rock, high above. I watch.

The dust settles. The two men stand amid the altered landscape. They are arguing. The younger one points wildly in several directions. The older one scans the slopes with a grim, methodical fury. They look at the path of the slide. They look at the untouched path beyond it. They do not look up.

They have lost the tracks. They have lost their quarry.

But they have found the sledge.

I see the older man approach it. He walks around the great, shrouded slab lashed to its frame of pine. He does not touch it. He simply looks. He understands what it is. He understands the magnitude of the labor. He looks from the sledge back up the mountain, his face unreadable at this distance.

Then he does something unexpected.

He nods. A single, slow dip of his head.

He turns. He says something to the younger man. He points down, toward the tree line. The younger man protests. The older man’s gesture becomes a command. Reluctantly, the younger one sheathes his sword. They begin to descend, leaving the sledge and its precious burden behind.

They are giving up.

But the older man’s nod was not defeat. It was acknowledgment. Respect, even.

He was not sent to retrieve the stone. Or to kill me for it.

He was sent to confirm.

To confirm that I had done the impossible. That I had won the stone from the mountain. That I had become the kind of man who could do such a thing.

And seeing it, his task was complete.

My brother did not send them to stop me.

He sent them to bear witness.

The cold that fills me then is the true cold of the undercroft. It climbs my spine like a slow vine. It is the cold of a calculation so deep, so patient, I cannot see its bottom.

He wanted me to have the stone. He wanted me to prove I could earn it. He wanted *them* to see that I had.

Why?

The hook in my spirit is no longer a twist. It is a lodestone, pointing down. Down the mountain, toward the tree line, toward the world.

Toward him.

I wait until the two men are specks, then swallowed by the green of the pines. The silence returns, deeper now, stained by their presence and their departure.

I descend back to my slab. I touch the rough, cool surface of the mold-stone. It is mine. It is his proof.

I have never felt more like a tool. A chisel in a hand I cannot see, striking a note I have not yet heard.

The child on the shoulders saw the sky first.

But the man who put him there controlled the view.

I rebuild the sledge. I take up the rope. The weight is the same. The weight is different. It is no longer the weight of the unmade world. It is the weight of a made one. A world shaped by my brother’s silent, ledger-keeping will.

I begin the final descent to the trees, to the roads, to the journey back.

The hollow in my chest is no longer quiet. It hums. It hums with the mountain’s note, and with a new, terrible understanding.

My brother remembers everything.

He has simply been composing a different song.



---

# Chapter 20

## CHAPTER 20

The world below the tree line is a different kind of deaf. The mountain’s silence was a roar of absence, a wind-scoured bowl of quiet. This silence is a held breath. The pines stand too close. Their needles drink sound, drink light, drink the space between thoughts. My boots on the needle-carpet make no sound worth the name. The sledge, that groaning beast of my ascent, is a ghost behind me. It whispers. It is the only voice left.

The mold-stone is a cold sun at my back. Its gravity bends the path. I feel it tugging at the rope, not down the slope, but sideways, toward some deeper magnetic pull. Toward him. My brother’s calculation is a scent on this still air. I follow it. I am the arrow he has spent a lifetime fletching, and now the bowstring is released. The hum in my chest is the sound of the arrow in flight.

I walk for a day, or the memory of a day. Time in the green gloom is a syrup, thick and distorting. The light does not change, only thickens. I eat the last of the hard cheese. It tastes of the cave, of my own sour fear. I drink from black streams that coil over moss-slick rock. The water tastes of pine and of iron. It tastes of the hook in my spirit, rusted and sure.

I do not sleep. Sleep is a room I have boarded up. Behind that door, the cold of the undercroft waits, and the lukewarm tooth of the stone against my shoulder, and the sweet-rot smell of the apple that was not there. To sleep is to remember. To remember is to walk into my brother’s ledger. I keep my eyes open. I watch the trees. They are all witnesses.

On the second day, or the third, the trees thin. The land gentles into a bruised valley. A road appears. Not a path worn by deer or desperate men, but a made thing. Its ruts are old, healed over with grass, but their geometry is a violence against the land. A straight line. A decision. I stand at its edge. The mold-stone on its sledge is an obscenity here. A god’s tooth spat onto a clerk’s desk.

The road leads east. East is where the city would be, if cities still stood. East is where the Abbey of the Ledger sits, a fortress of numbers and quiet. East is where he is. The lodestone in my gut vibrates, aligning itself. I step onto the road. The compacted earth is harder than the mountain rock. It gives nothing. It accepts nothing. It is a surface for traffic, not for truth.

I pull the sledge onto the road. The runners, silent on needles, now produce a low, constant moan. A song of friction. A song of arrival. I am announcing myself to the empty air. I am the note my brother composed. Let the world hear it.

The world, it seems, has been listening.

They emerge from the tree-shadows not as men, but as extensions of the gloom. Three of them. Their clothes are the color of mud and dusk. They do not carry themselves like the two on the mountain. Those were blades, clean and purposeful. These are knots. Complex, soiled, tied by need. They block the road, not with threat, but with presence. Their emptiness is the threat.

The one in front has a face like a knuckle. He looks at the sledge, at the shape under its oiled hides. He does not look at me. His eyes are for the thing I pull.

“That’s a heavy ghost,” he says. His voice is gravel in a tin cup.

I say nothing. The river of blood is in my ears again. But I have learned to listen between its beats. I hear the shift of a foot to the left. The catch of breath from the one on the right. They are not soldiers. They are hungry men. The mountain’s two witnesses were a question from my brother. These are an answer from the world. The world is always hungry.

“A man shouldn’t pull his own ghost,” says the knuckle-faced man. “Bad for the back. Let us ease your burden.”

“The burden is named,” I say. My voice is a stranger’s. It is the voice of the stone. “It does not wish for new hands.”

He smiles. It is a crack in the knuckle. “Everything wishes for new hands. Old hands get tired. They forget their grip.”

The one on the left is edging. Not toward me. Toward the back of the sledge. To cut the ropes. To let the weight do their work for them. They think the stone is treasure. They think it is silver, or tools, or salt. They think it is a thing that can be spent.

They are wrong. It is a thing that spends you.

I let go of the rope.

The action is so simple, so final, it stops them. The rope falls into the road dust like a dead snake. I take two steps back. I spread my hands, empty. “Take it.”

The knuckle-faced man blinks. His certainty, that fragile clay, shows a fissure. This is not in the scripture of robbery. The victim does not offer. He pleads, or he fights. He does not assent.

The one on the right, younger, with eyes like a startled hare, licks his lips. “A trick.”

“No trick,” I say. “You see the burden. You want it. It is yours. Pull it to your hearth. See what warmth it gives.”

The man on the left cannot help himself. He darts forward, a quick, furtive movement, and slices the hide ropes that bind the covers to the sledge with a small, dirty knife. He grabs the edge of the oiled hide and yanks it back.

The mold-stone is revealed.

In the mountain pass, it was a piece of the night. Here, on this civilized road, under this filtered green light, it is a hole. It does not reflect. It consumes. The light falls into it and does not return. Its surface is not smooth, but *final*. It is the shape left after all other shapes have been subtracted.

The three men stare. The knuckle-face forgets to breathe. The hare-eyed man takes a step back. The one with the knife lets the hide fall from his fingers. It whispers to the ground.

“What is it?” The question is a child’s whisper from the knuckle.

“It is a mold,” I say. “A mold for a world that did not happen. It is the shape of an ‘if’. It is very heavy.”

“It’s a devil-stone,” hisses the hare-eyed man. “It’s not right.”

“No,” I agree. “It is not right. It is a wrong thing. And now it is yours.”

I take another step back. I am a spectator. This is their transaction.

The knuckle-faced man’s greed wars with a deeper, animal sense. The greed is loud. It shouts of wealth, of rarity, of power over the man who gave it up so easily. The animal sense is a cold nose on the back of his neck. It smells the void. It smells the cold of the undercroft seeping from that perfect, black plane.

Greed wins. It always does. It is the older brother.

“Cover it,” he barks at the one with the knife. The man jumps, fumbles with the hide, and drapes it back over the stone. The act of covering it seems to restore some order. A hidden thing is a manageable thing.

“The sledge too,” says knuckle-face. “We take it all.”

They move around me, careful not to touch me. I am a carrier of plague, and they have taken the infected trunk. They are safe now. They grasp the rope. The two younger ones take the sides, ready to push. The knuckle puts his shoulder to the task.

They heave.

The sledge does not move.

They strain. A sound comes from the hare-eyed man, a grunt of pure effort. The man with the knife digs his heels into the ruts of the road. The knuckle-face snarls, his face purpling. He throws his whole weight forward.

The sledge remains. It is not that it is heavy. It is that it has *consented* to be moved by me. It has become part of my story. For them, it is just a stone. A stone that weighs as much as a world that never was.

They let go, panting. They look from the sledge to me. The fear is in them now, bright and sharp.

“What witchery is this?” the knuckle whispers, his voice stripped of its gravel.

“No witchery,” I say. I walk forward. They shrink back. I pick up the rope. I settle it across my shoulder, into the familiar groove of pain. I lean forward.

The sledge moves. The runners sigh through the dust. It moves for me. It is my burden. My proof. My sentence.

I look at the three men. Their hunger is gone. In its place is a holy terror. They have seen a law broken. The law that says a strong man can take what a weak man carries. I am not the weak man. The stone and I are a single, dreadful fact.

“You see?” I say. “Some ghosts only walk for the man who made them.”

I turn my back on them. I begin to walk east, down the straight line of the road. I do not look back. I listen. I hear no footsteps following. I hear only their stunned silence, and then, as the distance grows, the sound of one of them retching onto the grass.

I pull my ghost. The hum in my chest has changed. It is no longer just the mountain’s note, or the vibration of my brother’s will. It is the low, approving frequency of the mold-stone. It is pleased. It has been measured, and found wanting, and returned to its proper bearer.

The road unspools. The valley deepens. Signs of the world begin to bleed into the edges of the forest. A tumbledown wall, too straight to be natural. The blackened circle of an old cookfire. A piece of broken pottery, blue glaze faded to a memory. Then, a mile later, a post. A carved piece of wood, rotted at the base, leaning like a drunkard. On it, a symbol is burned, almost weathered away: a circle within a square. The sigil of the Abbey of the Ledger. A boundary marker.

I am on his land.

The recognition is a key turning in a lock I did not know I carried. The hollow in my chest yawns wide. It is not empty. It is filled with the cold, meticulous architecture of memory. Not my memory. His.

*He remembers everything.*

The child on the shoulders saw the sky first. But the man who put him there controlled the view.

My view now is this road, this valley, this leaning post. This is the view he has given me. The view of a petitioner approaching the throne. The throne of numbers. The throne of account.

A memory comes. Not a full vision, but a sensory splinter.

*The smell of wax and dust. The feel of vellum under my small, dirty fingers. I am under a table. A great oak table. His legs are beside me, the wool of his novice’s robe scratchy against my cheek. Above, the murmur of adult voices. The Prior’s dry cough. The scratch of a pen. My brother’s voice, young but already smoothed of all ripple. “The tithe from the western mill is seven parts in twelve of the expected. The deficit is recorded under three heads: damage to the wheel, sickness in the family, and an unseasonal frost. The frost is not our concern. The sickness is an expense against their future labor. The damage to the wheel is a capital debt. It must be entered thus.”*

*I did not understand the words. I understood the tone. It was the tone of a mason laying bricks. Each word a brick. Each sentence a course. He was building a wall. A wall of cause and effect, of debt and credit, of fact. A wall between us and the chaos of frosts and sickness and broken wheels. Under the table, I reached out and took his ankle. My hand was very small. He did not shake me off. He did not acknowledge me. His leg, within my grasp, was as still as a column. A part of the foundation.*

I stumble. The rope bites into my shoulder. The memory is a tripwire. It is not mine to recall. It is his. He has inserted it into me, like a page glued into my own book. A ledger entry in the story of my life. *Item: one moment of dependency. Value: to be determined.*

Is that what this is? A settling of accounts?

The day bleeds into a smoky twilight. I see a light ahead. Not the scatter of a village, but a single, steady point. A lantern on a pole. As I draw nearer, shapes resolve. A gatehouse. Low, built of the same grey stone as the mountain. A roof of mossy thatch. It is a tiny, swallowed piece of the Abbey, set here at the edge of things to taste all who approach.

Two figures stand in the lantern light. They wear the dun-colored robes of lay brothers. They do not carry weapons. They carry slates. And styli.

I do not stop pulling. The moan of the sledge runners is my herald. I walk until I am ten paces from the gate. The light falls on me, on the strange, huge shape I pull. It does not fall on the mold-stone. The stone drinks the light, creates a pool of deeper night at my back.

The two brothers look. Their eyes are not the eyes of the hungry men. They are not the eyes of the mountain witnesses. They are the eyes of clerks. They see a phenomenon. They begin to write on their slates. The scratch of stylus on stone is the only sound.

After a moment, the older of the two looks up. His face is long, weathered, impassive. “Name.”

It is not a question. It is a column heading.

“I am called Leo,” I say.

He writes. “Point of origin.”

“The mountain. The high pass.”

He writes. “Cargo.”

I am silent. How to enter this into a column? He looks up, waiting. His gaze is a dry sponge.

“A stone,” I say.

He writes. *One (1) stone.* “Purpose of entry onto Abbey demesne.”

This is the real question. The one my brother has written for them to ask.

I meet the clerk’s eyes. “To deliver a proof.”

He does not write immediately. He holds my gaze. In his eyes, I see no curiosity, no fear, no judgment. I see only a waiting cell. A cell in a table, ready to be filled with the correct data.

“Proof of what?” he asks.

“Of capability,” I say. “Of worth. Of a debt incurred.”

He nods, as if this is a common answer. *Proof: capability. Sub-category: worth. Linked to: debt (type: incurred).* He writes it down. The stylus whispers. He looks to the other brother, who has been writing my description. *Male. Adult. Signs of exposure. Garments: degraded. Pulling sledge (type: improvised).* The second brother gives a slight nod. The inventory is complete.
