I take my hand away.

On the surface of the dressed, perfect stone, a shape has appeared. It is not carved. It has risen, as if the stone remembered a texture it had long forgotten. The shape is simple. A single, branching line. An antler. Or a river. Or a lightning bolt. Or a root.

It is the first mark. The signature of the making.

Leo stumbles to the wall. He touches the mark. His fingers trace it. He is weeping, soundlessly. “It was… it was all true. It was all real. Not a phenomenon. A physiology.”

The Prior climbs to his feet. He is an old man, his certainty stripped away. He looks at the mark on the wall, then at me. “You have given their memory rest?”

“No,” I say. “I have given it work. I have broken the seal. The gift is moving again. It will not haunt you as a ghost. It will… infuse you. As a memory should. It will be in the warmth of the sun on the cloister wall. It will be in the taste of the bread. It will be in the quiet between heartbeats. The undercroft will be just a room again. Cold. Damp. Empty.”

“And you?” Leo asks, looking at me as if for the first time. Not as his vague sister. Not as a subject. As the maker, the rememberer.

I look at the flint knife. Its work is done. It is no longer an answer. It is just a piece of stone, beautifully made.

“I have been the vessel,” I say. “I have been the mold. I have held the old memory, and I have given it a new shape. A new passage into the world.” I feel a vast tiredness, but it is a good tiredness. The tiredness after a birth. “The story of the stag is loose now. It is in the stones. It is in the air. It is in you, brother, whether you model it or not.”

I walk past them, down the corridor. My clay-caked feet leave no more marks. The corridor accepts me now. I am not a stain. I am a part of its story.

“Where will you go?” the Prior calls after me, his voice stripped of authority. Only a question remains.

I do not turn. “To the kitchen,” I say. “I am hungry.”

It is true. A deep, specific, human hunger. For bread. For broth. For the warmth of a fire that cooks, not consecrates.

I come to the door that leads to the refectory, the kitchen, the world of simple, needful things. I pull the iron ring. It is cold. It is real.

I think of my brother, still in the corridor, touching the mark on the wall, his perfect models shattered around him, his mind alive with a new, terrifying, beautiful reality. He has his data now. It will consume him. It will remake him. He will spend his life trying to understand the gift, and in doing so, he will honor it.

He will never forget the stag.

I let the door close behind me. The hinge does not grate. It is well-oiled.

The kitchen is quiet, the great fire banked for the night. Embers glow. A pot of water hangs over them, kept warm. The smell is of rosemary, and onion, and ash.

I am coated in the earth’s gut. I hold a knife of stone.

I go to the hearth. I kneel before it. This hearth is for cooking, for life. Not for memory. But the principle is the same. A giving. A transformation.

I place the flint knife on the hearthstone. An offering to this new, humble fire.

I take a loaf of yesterday’s bread from the crock. I break it. The sound is sharp in the quiet. I dip it in the warm water. I eat.

It is the best thing I have ever tasted. It is not a memory. It is now. It is real.

As I eat, I feel it. A warmth, not from the embers. From within. It is the stag’s strength, moving in my blood. It is the patience of the stone, settling in my bones. It is the gratitude of the first maker, a quiet hum in my heart.

The gift has been given. It has been received.

I am not who I was. I am not the girl in the undercroft, building cold from memory. I am the woman who walked out of the deep dark, who remembered the making, and who gave the memory back to the world.

I am the bridge between the what was and the what is.

I finish the bread. I drink the warm, flavored water.

I sit by the hearth, in the quiet dark of the kitchen, and I wait for the dawn.

The stone of the hearth is warm against my back.

It is the right kind of warm.
